## Chapter: Exponential Growth & Decay

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Exponential Growth & Decay, a fundamental concept in Integrated Math 1. This chapter will delve into the fascinating world of exponential functions, their properties, and their real-world applications. 

Exponential growth and decay are mathematical concepts that describe the behavior of numerous natural and artificial processes. From the growth of populations to the decay of radioactive substances, the principles of exponential growth and decay are ubiquitous in our world. 

In this chapter, we will start by understanding the basic definition of exponential functions. We will explore the mathematical form of these functions, typically represented as $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$, where $a$ and $b$ are constants. We will learn how to identify the key components of an exponential function, such as the base, the exponent, and the constant factor.

We will then move on to the concepts of exponential growth and decay. We will learn how to distinguish between these two types of exponential functions based on the value of the base $b$. If $b > 1$, we have exponential growth, and if $0 < b < 1$, we have exponential decay. 

We will also delve into the graphical representation of exponential functions. We will learn how to plot these functions and interpret their graphs. We will understand how the graph of an exponential function can provide valuable insights into the behavior of the function over time.

Finally, we will explore some real-world applications of exponential growth and decay. We will see how these mathematical concepts can be used to model and predict various phenomena in fields such as biology, physics, economics, and more.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of exponential growth and decay, and you will be able to apply these concepts to solve a wide range of mathematical problems. So, let's embark on this exciting journey of learning and discovery.

## Chapter: Exponential growth & decay

### Section: Exponential vs. linear growth

In this section, we will compare and contrast exponential and linear growth. Understanding the differences between these two types of growth is crucial for interpreting and predicting the behavior of various mathematical and real-world phenomena.

#### Linear Growth

Linear growth is a simple and straightforward concept. In a linear growth model, the quantity of interest increases or decreases by a fixed amount over each unit of time. The mathematical representation of a linear function is $f(x) = mx + c$, where $m$ is the slope of the line (representing the rate of change), and $c$ is the y-intercept (representing the initial value).

The graph of a linear function is a straight line, which can slope upwards (for positive $m$) or downwards (for negative $m$). The slope of the line indicates the rate of growth or decay: a steeper slope corresponds to a faster rate of change.

#### Exponential Growth

As we have learned in the previous sections, exponential growth is a bit more complex. In an exponential growth model, the quantity of interest increases or decreases by a fixed percentage over each unit of time. The mathematical representation of an exponential function is $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$, where $a$ is the initial value, and $b$ is the base of the exponential (representing the growth factor).

The graph of an exponential function is a curve that becomes steeper and steeper as $x$ increases (for $b > 1$) or decreases (for $0 < b < 1$). This reflects the fact that the rate of change in an exponential function is not constant, but rather increases or decreases over time.

#### Comparing Exponential and Linear Growth

The key difference between linear and exponential growth lies in the rate of change. In linear growth, the rate of change is constant: the quantity increases or decreases by the same amount each time. In exponential growth, the rate of change is variable: the quantity increases or decreases by a larger amount each time.

This difference has significant implications for the behavior of the quantity over time. In a linear growth model, the quantity will increase or decrease steadily over time. In an exponential growth model, the quantity will increase or decrease rapidly, and this rate of change will accelerate over time.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into these differences and explore some examples of linear and exponential growth in real-world contexts. By understanding these concepts, you will be able to interpret and predict the behavior of various mathematical and real-world phenomena more effectively.

#### Understanding exponential growth and decay

Exponential growth and decay are two sides of the same coin. They both follow the same mathematical model, but with different values of $b$ in the function $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$. 

##### Exponential Growth

In exponential growth, the base $b$ is greater than 1. This means that the quantity of interest is increasing over time. The rate of increase is proportional to the current value of the quantity, which leads to a rapid acceleration in growth as time progresses. This is why the graph of an exponential growth function gets steeper and steeper as $x$ increases.

For example, if a population of bacteria doubles every hour, this is an example of exponential growth. If you start with one bacterium, after one hour you have two, after two hours you have four, after three hours you have eight, and so on. The number of bacteria is increasing by a factor of 2 each hour, so the function that models this growth is $f(x) = 1 \cdot 2^{x}$.

##### Exponential Decay

In exponential decay, the base $b$ is between 0 and 1. This means that the quantity of interest is decreasing over time. The rate of decrease is proportional to the current value of the quantity, which leads to a rapid deceleration in decay as time progresses. This is why the graph of an exponential decay function gets less and less steep as $x$ increases.

For example, if a radioactive substance loses half of its mass every year, this is an example of exponential decay. If you start with 100 grams of the substance, after one year you have 50 grams, after two years you have 25 grams, after three years you have 12.5 grams, and so on. The mass of the substance is decreasing by a factor of 0.5 each year, so the function that models this decay is $f(x) = 100 \cdot 0.5^{x}$.

##### Comparing Exponential Growth and Decay

The key difference between exponential growth and decay lies in the value of the base $b$. If $b > 1$, the function models exponential growth, and the quantity of interest increases over time. If $0 < b < 1$, the function models exponential decay, and the quantity of interest decreases over time.

In both cases, the rate of change is not constant, but rather proportional to the current value of the quantity. This leads to the characteristic "accelerating" shape of the graph of an exponential growth function, and the "decelerating" shape of the graph of an exponential decay function. 

Understanding these concepts is crucial for interpreting and predicting the behavior of various mathematical and real-world phenomena, from population growth and radioactive decay to compound interest and the spread of diseases.

### Section: Exponential expressions

Exponential expressions are mathematical expressions that involve an exponent. They are a fundamental part of understanding exponential growth and decay. 

#### Understanding exponential expressions

An exponential expression is written in the form $a^{b}$, where $a$ is the base and $b$ is the exponent. The base $a$ is the number that is being multiplied by itself, and the exponent $b$ tells us how many times the base is being multiplied.

For example, in the expression $2^{3}$, 2 is the base and 3 is the exponent. This expression means that we are multiplying 2 by itself 3 times, which equals 8.

Exponential expressions can also involve variables. For example, in the expression $2^{x}$, 2 is the base and $x$ is the exponent. This expression represents a function that multiplies 2 by itself $x$ times. 

##### Evaluating exponential expressions

To evaluate an exponential expression, you multiply the base by itself the number of times indicated by the exponent. 

For example, to evaluate $3^{4}$, you multiply 3 by itself 4 times: $3 \times 3 \times 3 \times 3 = 81$.

If the exponent is a variable, you can substitute the value of the variable into the expression to evaluate it. For example, if $x = 2$ in the expression $3^{x}$, you substitute 2 for $x$ and multiply 3 by itself 2 times: $3 \times 3 = 9$.

##### Properties of exponential expressions

Exponential expressions have several important properties that can help simplify calculations:

1. **Product of powers property**: $a^{m} \cdot a^{n} = a^{m+n}$. When you multiply two powers with the same base, you can add the exponents.

2. **Quotient of powers property**: $a^{m} / a^{n} = a^{m-n}$. When you divide two powers with the same base, you can subtract the exponents.

3. **Power of a power property**: $(a^{m})^{n} = a^{mn}$. When you raise a power to another power, you can multiply the exponents.

4. **Zero exponent property**: $a^{0} = 1$. Any number (except 0) raised to the power of 0 is 1.

5. **Negative exponent property**: $a^{-n} = 1/a^{n}$. A negative exponent means that the base is on the wrong side of the fraction line, so you need to flip the base to the other side.

Understanding these properties can help you manipulate and simplify exponential expressions, which is a key skill in working with exponential growth and decay.

```
### Section: Simplifying exponential expressions

Simplifying exponential expressions is a crucial skill in understanding and solving problems related to exponential growth and decay. This section will guide you through the process of simplifying exponential expressions using the properties of exponents we discussed in the previous section.

#### Simplifying exponential expressions with the same base

When simplifying exponential expressions with the same base, we can use the product of powers property and the quotient of powers property.

For example, consider the expression $2^{3} \cdot 2^{4}$. Using the product of powers property, we can add the exponents to simplify the expression: $2^{3+4} = 2^{7} = 128$.

Similarly, for the expression $5^{6} / 5^{2}$, we can use the quotient of powers property to subtract the exponents: $5^{6-2} = 5^{4} = 625$.

#### Simplifying exponential expressions with the same exponent

When simplifying exponential expressions with the same exponent, we can use the power of a power property.

For example, consider the expression $(2^{3})^{4}$. Using the power of a power property, we can multiply the exponents to simplify the expression: $2^{3 \cdot 4} = 2^{12} = 4096$.

#### Simplifying exponential expressions with a zero exponent

Any number (except 0) raised to the power of 0 is 1. This is known as the zero exponent property. 

For example, $7^{0} = 1$, $(-3)^{0} = 1$, and $(2x)^{0} = 1$.

#### Simplifying exponential expressions with negative exponents

A negative exponent means that the base is on the wrong side of the fraction line, so you need to flip the base to the other side. For example, $2^{-3}$ is the same as $1/2^{3}$ or $1/8$.

#### Practice Problems

1. Simplify the expression $3^{4} \cdot 3^{2}$.
2. Simplify the expression $6^{5} / 6^{3}$.
3. Simplify the expression $(4^{2})^{3}$.
4. Simplify the expression $9^{0}$.
5. Simplify the expression $5^{-2}$.

Remember, practice is key to mastering the simplification of exponential expressions. Try to solve these problems and check your answers with the solutions provided at the end of the chapter.
```

## Chapter: Exponential growth & decay

### Section: Graphs of exponential growth

Understanding the graphs of exponential functions is crucial in mastering the concept of exponential growth. This section will guide you through the process of graphing exponential functions and interpreting these graphs.

#### Graphing exponential functions

An exponential function can be written in the form $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$, where $a$ and $b$ are constants, $b > 0$, $b \neq 1$, and $x$ is any real number. 

The graph of an exponential function is a curve that either increases or decreases rapidly depending on the value of the base $b$. If $b > 1$, the function represents exponential growth and the graph will be increasing. If $0 < b < 1$, the function represents exponential decay and the graph will be decreasing.

Let's consider the function $f(x) = 2^{x}$. This is an exponential growth function because the base $2$ is greater than $1$. 

To graph this function, we can create a table of values for $x$ and $f(x)$:

| $x$ | $f(x)$ |
| --- | ------ |
| -2  | 0.25   |
| -1  | 0.5    |
| 0   | 1      |
| 1   | 2      |
| 2   | 4      |

Plotting these points and drawing a curve through them will give us the graph of the function $f(x) = 2^{x}$.

#### Interpreting graphs of exponential functions

The graph of an exponential function has several key features:

- The y-intercept is at $(0, a)$, where $a$ is the coefficient in the function.
- If the base $b > 1$, the graph increases as $x$ increases and decreases as $x$ decreases. The graph will also approach but never touch the x-axis as $x$ decreases, creating a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$.
- If $0 < b < 1$, the graph decreases as $x$ increases and increases as $x$ decreases. The graph will also approach but never touch the x-axis as $x$ increases, creating a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$.

#### Practice Problems

1. Graph the function $f(x) = 3^{x}$.
2. Graph the function $f(x) = (1/2)^{x}$.
3. For the function $f(x) = 5 \cdot 2^{x}$, identify the y-intercept and describe the behavior of the graph as $x$ increases and decreases.
4. For the function $f(x) = 4 \cdot (1/3)^{x}$, identify the y-intercept and describe the behavior of the graph as $x$ increases and decreases.

Remember, practice is key to mastering the graphing and interpretation of exponential functions.

```
#### Interpreting graphs of exponential functions (continued)

In the previous section, we discussed the key features of the graph of an exponential function. Now, let's delve deeper into interpreting these graphs.

##### The y-intercept

The y-intercept of an exponential function is the point where the graph intersects the y-axis. This point is always $(0, a)$, where $a$ is the coefficient in the function. For example, in the function $f(x) = 2^{x}$, the y-intercept is at $(0, 1)$ because when $x = 0$, $f(x) = 1$.

##### The horizontal asymptote

The horizontal asymptote of an exponential function is a horizontal line that the graph approaches but never touches. For exponential growth functions ($b > 1$), the graph approaches the x-axis as $x$ decreases, creating a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$. For exponential decay functions ($0 < b < 1$), the graph approaches the x-axis as $x$ increases, also creating a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$.

##### The rate of growth or decay

The rate of growth or decay of an exponential function is determined by the base $b$. If $b > 1$, the function is growing, and the larger the base, the faster the function grows. If $0 < b < 1$, the function is decaying, and the closer the base is to $0$, the faster the function decays.

##### The effect of the coefficient $a$

The coefficient $a$ in the function $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$ affects the position of the graph. If $a > 1$, the graph is shifted upward, and if $0 < a < 1$, the graph is shifted downward. The coefficient $a$ does not affect the rate of growth or decay.

#### Practice Problems

1. For the function $f(x) = 3^{x}$, identify the y-intercept, the horizontal asymptote, and the rate of growth.
2. For the function $f(x) = (1/2)^{x}$, identify the y-intercept, the horizontal asymptote, and the rate of decay.
3. For the function $f(x) = 5 \cdot 2^{x}$, identify the y-intercept, the horizontal asymptote, and the rate of growth. How does the coefficient $5$ affect the graph?
4. For the function $f(x) = 0.5 \cdot 3^{x}$, identify the y-intercept, the horizontal asymptote, and the rate of growth. How does the coefficient $0.5$ affect the graph?
```

### Section: Exponential vs. linear growth over time

In this section, we will compare exponential and linear growth over time. Understanding the difference between these two types of growth is crucial in many fields, including economics, biology, and physics.

#### Linear Growth

Linear growth is the simplest type of growth. In a linear growth model, the quantity increases by a fixed amount over each unit of time. The function that represents linear growth is of the form $f(x) = mx + b$, where $m$ is the slope of the line (representing the rate of growth), and $b$ is the y-intercept.

For example, if you save $100$ dollars every month, the total amount of money you have saved over time grows linearly. After one month, you have $100$ dollars, after two months, you have $200$ dollars, and so on.

#### Exponential Growth

Exponential growth, on the other hand, is a type of growth where the quantity increases by a fixed percentage over each unit of time. The function that represents exponential growth is of the form $f(x) = a \cdot b^{x}$, where $a$ is the initial amount, and $b$ is the growth factor.

For example, if you invest $100$ dollars in a bank account that gives you a $5\%$ interest every month, the amount of money in your account grows exponentially. After one month, you have $105$ dollars, after two months, you have $110.25$ dollars, and so on.

#### Comparing Exponential and Linear Growth over Time

When comparing exponential and linear growth over time, one of the most noticeable differences is the rate at which the quantity increases. In linear growth, the rate of increase is constant, while in exponential growth, the rate of increase becomes faster over time.

Let's consider the previous examples. In the linear growth model, you save a constant amount of $100$ dollars every month. In the exponential growth model, the amount of money in your account increases by $5\%$ every month. After $10$ months, in the linear model, you would have $1000$ dollars, while in the exponential model, you would have approximately $1629$ dollars.

This difference becomes even more pronounced over longer periods. After $100$ months, in the linear model, you would have $10,000$ dollars, while in the exponential model, you would have approximately $131,501$ dollars.

In conclusion, while both linear and exponential growth models can describe increasing quantities, the rate at which these quantities increase is vastly different. Understanding these differences is crucial in many real-world applications.

#### Practice Problems

1. For the function $f(x) = 5x + 2$, identify the slope and the y-intercept. What do these values represent in terms of growth?
2. For the function $f(x) = 3 \cdot (1.05)^{x}$, identify the initial amount and the growth factor. What do these values represent in terms of growth?
3. Compare the amount of money you would have after $50$ months in a savings account where you deposit $200$ dollars every month (linear growth), and in a bank account where you initially deposit $200$ dollars and receive a $2\%$ interest every month (exponential growth).

e in the exponential model, you would have approximately $1629$ dollars. This is because the amount of money increases by a larger amount each month in the exponential model.

### Section: Applying exponential and linear growth in real-world contexts

In this section, we will explore how exponential and linear growth models can be applied in real-world contexts. These models are not just theoretical constructs, but have practical applications in various fields.

#### Population Growth

One of the most common applications of exponential growth is in modeling population growth. If a population has a constant birth rate and no death rate, it will grow exponentially. For example, if a bacteria colony doubles every hour, the number of bacteria can be modeled by the function $f(x) = 2^x$, where $x$ is the number of hours.

On the other hand, if a population increases by a fixed number each year, it can be modeled by a linear growth function. For example, if a town's population increases by 500 people each year, the population can be modeled by the function $f(x) = 500x + P_0$, where $x$ is the number of years and $P_0$ is the initial population.

#### Economic Growth

Economic growth can also be modeled using exponential and linear functions. If an economy grows by a fixed percentage each year, it can be modeled by an exponential function. For example, if a country's GDP grows by 2% each year, the GDP can be modeled by the function $f(x) = GDP_0 \cdot 1.02^x$, where $x$ is the number of years and $GDP_0$ is the initial GDP.

On the other hand, if an economy grows by a fixed amount each year, it can be modeled by a linear function. For example, if a country's GDP increases by $1$ trillion dollars each year, the GDP can be modeled by the function $f(x) = 1 \times 10^{12}x + GDP_0$, where $x$ is the number of years and $GDP_0$ is the initial GDP.

In conclusion, understanding the difference between exponential and linear growth and knowing how to apply these models in real-world contexts is a crucial skill in many fields. Whether you're studying biology, economics, or just trying to plan your savings, these concepts can help you make sense of the world around you.

### Section: Exponential growth & decay

Exponential growth and decay are two important concepts in mathematics that describe how quantities change over time. They are used in a variety of fields, from biology and economics to physics and engineering. 

#### Understanding exponential growth and decay

Exponential growth occurs when the rate of change of a quantity is proportional to the current amount. This means that as the quantity increases, the rate at which it increases also increases. This is often seen in populations of organisms, financial investments, and in the spread of diseases.

Mathematically, exponential growth can be represented by the function $f(x) = a \cdot b^x$, where $a$ is the initial amount, $b$ is the growth factor, and $x$ is the time. If $b > 1$, the function represents exponential growth.

For example, if a population of bacteria doubles every hour, the population after $x$ hours can be modeled by the function $f(x) = 2^x$. Here, $a = 1$ (the initial population) and $b = 2$ (the growth factor).

Exponential decay, on the other hand, occurs when the rate of change of a quantity is proportional to the current amount, but in this case, the quantity decreases over time. This is often seen in radioactive decay, depreciation of assets, and cooling of objects.

Mathematically, exponential decay can be represented by the function $f(x) = a \cdot b^x$, where $a$ is the initial amount, $0 < b < 1$ is the decay factor, and $x$ is the time.

For example, if a radioactive substance halves every day, the amount of substance remaining after $x$ days can be modeled by the function $f(x) = \frac{1}{2}^x$. Here, $a = 1$ (the initial amount) and $b = \frac{1}{2}$ (the decay factor).

In conclusion, understanding exponential growth and decay is crucial for modeling various real-world phenomena. In the next sections, we will explore more about these concepts and learn how to solve problems involving exponential growth and decay.

### Section: Modeling exponential growth and decay

In the previous section, we introduced the concepts of exponential growth and decay and their mathematical representations. Now, we will delve deeper into how these concepts can be used to model real-world phenomena.

#### Subsection: Modeling exponential growth

Let's start with an example of exponential growth. Suppose you invest $1000 in a bank account that offers an annual interest rate of 5%. This interest is compounded annually, meaning that each year, the interest is added to the principal, and the next year's interest is calculated based on this new total.

The amount of money in the account after $x$ years can be modeled by the function $f(x) = 1000 \cdot (1 + 0.05)^x$. Here, $a = 1000$ is the initial amount, $b = 1.05$ is the growth factor (1 plus the interest rate), and $x$ is the time in years.

This function shows that the amount of money in the account grows exponentially over time. After one year, the account balance will be $1050. After two years, it will be $1102.50, and so on.

#### Subsection: Modeling exponential decay

Now, let's consider an example of exponential decay. Suppose you have a radioactive substance with a half-life of 5 days. This means that every 5 days, half of the substance decays.

The amount of substance remaining after $x$ days can be modeled by the function $f(x) = 1 \cdot (0.5)^{x/5}$. Here, $a = 1$ is the initial amount, $b = 0.5$ is the decay factor (representing the half-life), and $x$ is the time in days.

This function shows that the amount of substance decreases exponentially over time. After 5 days, half of the substance will have decayed. After 10 days, only a quarter will remain, and so on.

In conclusion, exponential growth and decay models are powerful tools for understanding and predicting how quantities change over time. In the next sections, we will learn how to solve problems involving these models.

### Section: Exponential functions from tables & graphs

In the previous sections, we learned about exponential growth and decay and how to model them using mathematical functions. Now, we will learn how to identify exponential functions from tables and graphs. This skill is crucial in understanding and interpreting real-world data.

#### Subsection: Identifying exponential functions from tables

When given a table of values, how can we tell if it represents an exponential function? There are a few key characteristics to look for:

1. **Constant Ratio:** In an exponential function, the ratio between consecutive outputs (y-values) is constant. This is because each output is obtained by multiplying the previous output by a fixed factor. For example, consider the following table:

| x | f(x) |
|---|------|
| 0 | 1    |
| 1 | 2    |
| 2 | 4    |
| 3 | 8    |
| 4 | 16   |

In this table, each output is twice the previous output, so the ratio between consecutive outputs is constant (2). This indicates that the function is exponential.

2. **Non-constant Differences:** Unlike linear functions, where the difference between consecutive outputs is constant, in an exponential function, this difference is not constant. In the table above, the differences between consecutive outputs are 1, 2, 4, and 8, which are not constant.

3. **Growth or Decay:** Exponential functions represent either growth or decay. If the ratio between consecutive outputs is greater than 1, the function represents exponential growth. If the ratio is between 0 and 1, it represents exponential decay.

In the next subsection, we will learn how to identify exponential functions from graphs.

#### Identifying exponential functions from graphs

Just like tables, graphs can also be used to identify exponential functions. Here are some key characteristics to look for:

1. **Curved Line:** The graph of an exponential function is a curved line, not a straight line. This is because the rate of change in an exponential function is not constant. The curve will be upward for exponential growth and downward for exponential decay.

2. **Passes Through (0,1):** For an exponential function of the form $f(x) = a \cdot b^x$, where $a$ and $b$ are positive constants and $b \neq 1$, the graph will always pass through the point (0,1) if $a=1$. This is because any number raised to the power of 0 is 1.

3. **Horizontal Asymptote:** The graph of an exponential function has a horizontal asymptote. This is a horizontal line that the graph approaches but never touches or crosses. For exponential growth, the asymptote is the x-axis (y=0). For exponential decay, the asymptote could be any line y=c, where c is the initial value of the function.

4. **Growth or Decay:** As with tables, exponential functions represent either growth or decay. If the graph is rising from left to right, it represents exponential growth. If it is falling from left to right, it represents exponential decay.

Let's consider an example. The graph below represents the function $f(x) = 2^x$.

![Exponential Function Graph](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Exponential_Function_%282%5Ex%29.svg/1200px-Exponential_Function_%282%5Ex%29.svg.png)

This graph has all the characteristics of an exponential function:

- It is a curved line.
- It passes through the point (0,1).
- It has a horizontal asymptote at y=0.
- It is rising from left to right, indicating exponential growth.

In the next section, we will learn how to use these characteristics to solve problems involving exponential functions.

### Section: Exponential vs. linear models

In the previous section, we learned how to identify exponential functions from graphs. Now, let's compare exponential models with linear models. Understanding the differences between these two types of models is crucial for choosing the right model for a given situation.

#### Linear Models

Linear models are used when the rate of change is constant. In other words, the amount of change in the dependent variable (usually denoted as $y$) for each unit change in the independent variable (usually denoted as $x$) is the same. The general form of a linear function is $y = mx + b$, where $m$ is the slope of the line (representing the rate of change) and $b$ is the y-intercept (representing the initial value).

The graph of a linear function is a straight line. The slope of the line indicates whether the function represents growth (if the slope is positive) or decay (if the slope is negative).

#### Exponential Models

As we have learned, exponential models are used when the rate of change is not constant but increases or decreases at a rate proportional to the current value. The general form of an exponential function is $f(x) = a \cdot b^x$, where $a$ is the initial value and $b$ is the growth factor if $b > 1$ (representing exponential growth) or the decay factor if $0 < b < 1$ (representing exponential decay).

The graph of an exponential function is a curved line. If the function represents exponential growth, the graph rises from left to right. If the function represents exponential decay, the graph falls from left to right.

#### Comparing Exponential and Linear Models

When comparing exponential and linear models, it's important to consider the context of the problem. If the rate of change is constant, a linear model is appropriate. If the rate of change is proportional to the current value, an exponential model is appropriate.

For example, if you are saving money and add the same amount to your savings account every month, this represents a linear model because the amount of money in your account increases at a constant rate. On the other hand, if you invest money and earn interest that is compounded, this represents an exponential model because the amount of money in your account increases at a rate proportional to the current amount.

In the next section, we will learn how to use exponential and linear models to solve real-world problems.

#### Choosing the appropriate model for a given situation

In real-world scenarios, it's not always immediately clear whether a linear or exponential model is the most appropriate. However, understanding the characteristics of each can guide us in making the right choice.

Let's continue with the example of saving money. If you add the same amount to your savings account every month, the amount of money in your account increases at a constant rate. This is a linear growth situation, and it can be modeled with a linear function of the form $y = mx + b$, where $m$ is the amount of money you add each month, and $b$ is the initial amount in your account.

On the other hand, suppose you have an investment that increases in value by a certain percentage each year. In this case, the amount of money in your account is not increasing by a constant amount. Instead, the amount of increase is proportional to the current amount in the account. This is an exponential growth situation, and it can be modeled with an exponential function of the form $f(x) = a \cdot b^x$, where $a$ is the initial amount in your account, and $b$ is the growth factor.

In general, if the rate of change is constant, a linear model is the best choice. If the rate of change is proportional to the current value, an exponential model is more appropriate.

However, keep in mind that real-world situations can be complex, and sometimes neither a purely linear nor a purely exponential model will be a perfect fit. In such cases, it may be necessary to use a combination of models or to use more advanced mathematical techniques. The key is to understand the underlying processes and to choose the model that best represents those processes. 

In the next section, we will explore more examples of situations that can be modeled using either linear or exponential functions, and we will practice choosing the most appropriate model.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of exponential growth and decay. We have learned that these two concepts are fundamental to understanding many natural and man-made phenomena. From the growth of populations to the decay of radioactive substances, exponential growth and decay models provide us with the tools to predict future outcomes based on current conditions.

We started by defining exponential growth and decay, and then moved on to understanding their mathematical representations. We learned that the key to understanding these concepts lies in the base of the exponential function. A base greater than 1 represents growth, while a base between 0 and 1 represents decay.

We also discussed the importance of the initial value in these models. This value, often denoted by $P_0$ or $A_0$, represents the starting point of the process. It is from this point that the process either grows or decays.

Finally, we delved into the practical applications of exponential growth and decay. We saw how these models can be used to predict the future size of a population, the amount of a substance that will remain after a certain period of time, and the value of an investment.

Remember, the power of exponential growth and decay lies in their ability to model real-world situations. By understanding these concepts, you are better equipped to make predictions and informed decisions in a variety of fields.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 2

#### Exercise 3
Using the exponential growth model from Exercise 1, predict the population of the bacteria after 12 hours.

#### Exercise 4
Using the exponential decay model from Exercise 2, predict the amount of the radioactive substance that will remain after 7 days.

#### Exercise 5
An investment grows at a rate of 5% per year. If the initial investment is $1000, write an exponential growth model to represent this situation. Using this model, predict the value of the investment after 10 years.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of exponential growth and decay. We have learned that these two concepts are fundamental to understanding many natural and man-made phenomena. From the growth of populations to the decay of radioactive substances, exponential growth and decay models provide us with the tools to predict future outcomes based on current conditions.

We started by defining exponential growth and decay, and then moved on to understanding their mathematical representations. We learned that the key to understanding these concepts lies in the base of the exponential function. A base greater than 1 represents growth, while a base between 0 and 1 represents decay.

We also discussed the importance of the initial value in these models. This value, often denoted by $P_0$ or $A_0$, represents the starting point of the process. It is from this point that the process either grows or decays.

Finally, we delved into the practical applications of exponential growth and decay. We saw how these models can be used to predict the future size of a population, the amount of a substance that will remain after a certain period of time, and the value of an investment.

Remember, the power of exponential growth and decay lies in their ability to model real-world situations. By understanding these concepts, you are better equipped to make predictions and informed decisions in a variety of fields.

## Chapter: Performing transformations

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on "Performing transformations". This chapter is designed to provide you with a comprehensive understanding of transformations in the context of Integrated Math 1. Transformations are a fundamental concept in mathematics, and they play a crucial role in various fields, including geometry, algebra, and calculus. 

In this chapter, we will delve into the different types of transformations, namely translations, rotations, reflections, and dilations. Each type of transformation alters the position, orientation, or size of a figure in a specific way. For instance, a translation moves a figure along a straight path in a particular direction, while a rotation turns a figure around a fixed point.

We will also explore the properties of transformations, such as the preservation of distance and angles, which make them particularly useful in solving a wide range of mathematical problems. Furthermore, we will discuss the concept of transformation matrices, which provide a powerful tool for performing transformations in a systematic and efficient manner.

The chapter will be filled with numerous examples and exercises to help you grasp the concepts and apply them in practical situations. By the end of this chapter, you should be able to perform transformations with confidence and understand their significance in the broader context of mathematics.

Remember, the key to mastering transformations, like any other mathematical concept, lies in practice and understanding. So, let's embark on this exciting journey of learning transformations in Integrated Math 1.

## Chapter: Performing transformations

### Section: Translations

#### Understanding translations

In the world of mathematics, a translation is a type of transformation that moves every point of a figure or a shape the same distance in the same direction. It's like sliding a figure without rotating or flipping it. The original figure and its translation have the same shape and size, and they face the same direction.

To understand translations better, let's consider an example. Suppose we have a point $A$ at coordinates $(x, y)$ and we want to translate it to a new position $A'$ by moving it $p$ units to the right and $q$ units up. The new coordinates of $A'$ would be $(x+p, y+q)$. 

In other words, if we have a point $(x, y)$ and we translate it by a vector $\vec{v} = (p, q)$, the translated point is given by $(x+p, y+q)$. 

This concept can be extended to translate any figure, not just a single point. For instance, if we want to translate a triangle with vertices at $(x_1, y_1)$, $(x_2, y_2)$, and $(x_3, y_3)$ by the vector $\vec{v} = (p, q)$, the translated triangle will have vertices at $(x_1+p, y_1+q)$, $(x_2+p, y_2+q)$, and $(x_3+p, y_3+q)$.

#### Properties of Translations

Translations have some important properties that are worth noting:

1. **Preservation of Shape and Size**: A translation does not change the shape or size of a figure. This means that the original figure and its image after translation are congruent.

2. **Preservation of Orientation**: A translation does not change the orientation of a figure. This means that the figure does not turn or flip during a translation.

3. **Preservation of Distances and Angles**: A translation preserves distances and angles. This means that the lengths of sides and measures of angles remain the same after a translation.

4. **Additivity**: The sum of two translations is another translation. If a point is translated by vector $\vec{v}$ and then by vector $\vec{w}$, it's the same as if it were translated by the vector $\vec{v} + \vec{w}$.

In the next section, we will delve into the practical application of translations, where we will learn how to perform translations on various geometric figures. We will also explore how to represent translations using transformation matrices. Stay tuned!

#### Performing translations on the coordinate plane

Now that we have a good understanding of what translations are and their properties, let's delve into how to perform translations on the coordinate plane.

##### Translating a Point

Translating a point on the coordinate plane is straightforward. If we have a point $A$ at coordinates $(x, y)$ and we want to translate it by a vector $\vec{v} = (p, q)$, we simply add $p$ to the $x$-coordinate and $q$ to the $y$-coordinate. The translated point $A'$ will then be at the coordinates $(x+p, y+q)$.

##### Translating a Figure

Translating a figure, such as a triangle or a rectangle, involves translating each of its vertices. Suppose we have a triangle with vertices at $(x_1, y_1)$, $(x_2, y_2)$, and $(x_3, y_3)$ and we want to translate it by a vector $\vec{v} = (p, q)$. We add $p$ to each $x$-coordinate and $q$ to each $y$-coordinate. The translated triangle will then have vertices at $(x_1+p, y_1+q)$, $(x_2+p, y_2+q)$, and $(x_3+p, y_3+q)$.

##### Example

Let's consider an example to illustrate these concepts. Suppose we have a rectangle with vertices at $(1, 2)$, $(1, 5)$, $(4, 2)$, and $(4, 5)$ and we want to translate it 3 units to the right and 2 units up. The translation vector is $\vec{v} = (3, 2)$. 

Applying the translation to each vertex, we get the new vertices at $(1+3, 2+2) = (4, 4)$, $(1+3, 5+2) = (4, 7)$, $(4+3, 2+2) = (7, 4)$, and $(4+3, 5+2) = (7, 7)$. The translated rectangle now has vertices at $(4, 4)$, $(4, 7)$, $(7, 4)$, and $(7, 7)$.

Remember, the shape, size, and orientation of the rectangle remain the same after the translation. Only its position on the coordinate plane has changed.

In the next section, we will explore another type of transformation: rotations.

#### Understanding rotations

After understanding translations, let's now delve into another type of transformation: rotations. In mathematics, a rotation is a transformation that turns a figure around a fixed point, known as the center of rotation. The amount of rotation is usually expressed in degrees.

##### Rotating a Point

To rotate a point $P$ at coordinates $(x, y)$ around the origin $(0, 0)$ by an angle $\theta$, we can use the following formulas:

$$
x' = x \cos(\theta) - y \sin(\theta)
$$
$$
y' = x \sin(\theta) + y \cos(\theta)
$$

Here, $(x', y')$ are the coordinates of the rotated point $P'$. Note that the angle $\theta$ is measured counterclockwise from the positive $x$-axis.

##### Rotating a Figure

Just like translations, rotating a figure involves rotating each of its vertices. Suppose we have a triangle with vertices at $(x_1, y_1)$, $(x_2, y_2)$, and $(x_3, y_3)$ and we want to rotate it by an angle $\theta$ around the origin. We apply the rotation formulas to each vertex to get the new vertices $(x_1', y_1')$, $(x_2', y_2')$, and $(x_3', y_3')$.

##### Example

Let's consider an example to illustrate these concepts. Suppose we have a rectangle with vertices at $(1, 2)$, $(1, 5)$, $(4, 2)$, and $(4, 5)$ and we want to rotate it 90 degrees counterclockwise around the origin. 

Applying the rotation formulas to each vertex, we get the new vertices at $(-2, 1)$, $(-5, 1)$, $(-2, 4)$, and $(-5, 4)$. The rotated rectangle now has vertices at $(-2, 1)$, $(-5, 1)$, $(-2, 4)$, and $(-5, 4)$.

Remember, the shape and size of the rectangle remain the same after the rotation. Only its position and orientation on the coordinate plane have changed.

In the next section, we will explore another type of transformation: reflections.

### Section: Rotations

#### Subsection: Performing rotations on the coordinate plane

In the previous section, we learned about the concept of rotations and how to rotate a point or a figure around the origin. Now, let's put this knowledge into practice and learn how to perform rotations on the coordinate plane.

##### Rotating a Point on the Coordinate Plane

To rotate a point on the coordinate plane, follow these steps:

1. Identify the point $P$ with coordinates $(x, y)$ that you want to rotate.
2. Determine the angle $\theta$ by which you want to rotate the point. Remember, this angle is measured counterclockwise from the positive $x$-axis.
3. Apply the rotation formulas to calculate the new coordinates $(x', y')$ of the rotated point $P'$:

$$
x' = x \cos(\theta) - y \sin(\theta)
$$
$$
y' = x \sin(\theta) + y \cos(\theta)
$$

4. Plot the new point $P'$ on the coordinate plane.

##### Rotating a Figure on the Coordinate Plane

To rotate a figure on the coordinate plane, follow these steps:

1. Identify the vertices of the figure that you want to rotate.
2. Determine the angle $\theta$ by which you want to rotate the figure.
3. Apply the rotation formulas to each vertex to calculate the new vertices of the rotated figure.
4. Connect the new vertices to form the rotated figure.

##### Example

Let's consider an example to illustrate these steps. Suppose we have a point $P$ at $(3, 4)$ and we want to rotate it 180 degrees counterclockwise around the origin.

Applying the rotation formulas, we get:

$$
x' = 3 \cos(180^\circ) - 4 \sin(180^\circ) = -3
$$
$$
y' = 3 \sin(180^\circ) + 4 \cos(180^\circ) = -4
$$

So, the rotated point $P'$ is at $(-3, -4)$.

Remember, the process of rotation does not change the distance of the point from the origin. It only changes the position of the point on the coordinate plane.

In the next section, we will continue our exploration of transformations by learning about reflections.

### Section: Reflections

#### Subsection: Understanding reflections

In the previous sections, we have learned about translations and rotations, two types of transformations that can be performed on the coordinate plane. Now, we will explore another type of transformation called a reflection.

A reflection is a transformation that flips a figure over a line, which we call the line of reflection. The figure and its reflection are mirror images of each other. Each point and its reflected point are the same distance from the line of reflection.

#### Reflecting a Point on the Coordinate Plane

To reflect a point on the coordinate plane, follow these steps:

1. Identify the point $P$ with coordinates $(x, y)$ that you want to reflect.
2. Determine the line of reflection. This could be any line, but for simplicity, we will focus on reflections over the $x$-axis and the $y$-axis.
3. Apply the reflection formulas to calculate the new coordinates $(x', y')$ of the reflected point $P'$:

   - If the line of reflection is the $x$-axis, then $x' = x$ and $y' = -y$.
   - If the line of reflection is the $y$-axis, then $x' = -x$ and $y' = y$.

4. Plot the new point $P'$ on the coordinate plane.

#### Reflecting a Figure on the Coordinate Plane

To reflect a figure on the coordinate plane, follow these steps:

1. Identify the vertices of the figure that you want to reflect.
2. Determine the line of reflection.
3. Apply the reflection formulas to each vertex to calculate the new vertices of the reflected figure.
4. Connect the new vertices to form the reflected figure.

#### Example

Let's consider an example to illustrate these steps. Suppose we have a point $P$ at $(3, 4)$ and we want to reflect it over the $x$-axis.

Applying the reflection formula, we get:

$$
x' = 3
$$
$$
y' = -4
$$

So, the reflected point $P'$ is at $(3, -4)$.

Remember, the process of reflection does not change the distance of the point from the line of reflection. It only changes the position of the point on the coordinate plane.

In the next section, we will continue our exploration of transformations by learning about dilations.

### Section: Reflections

#### Subsection: Performing reflections on the coordinate plane

Now that we have a basic understanding of what reflections are and how they work, let's dive deeper into performing reflections on the coordinate plane. 

##### Reflecting a Line on the Coordinate Plane

Reflecting a line on the coordinate plane involves reflecting each point on the line. Here are the steps:

1. Identify the line that you want to reflect. This could be any line, but for simplicity, we will focus on lines that are either vertical or horizontal.
2. Determine the line of reflection.
3. Apply the reflection formulas to each point on the line to calculate the new points of the reflected line.
4. Connect the new points to form the reflected line.

##### Example

Let's consider an example to illustrate these steps. Suppose we have a line segment with endpoints $A$ at $(2, 3)$ and $B$ at $(5, 3)$ and we want to reflect it over the $x$-axis.

Applying the reflection formula to point $A$, we get:

$$
x' = 2
$$
$$
y' = -3
$$

So, the reflected point $A'$ is at $(2, -3)$.

Applying the reflection formula to point $B$, we get:

$$
x' = 5
$$
$$
y' = -3
$$

So, the reflected point $B'$ is at $(5, -3)$.

Connecting points $A'$ and $B'$, we get the reflected line segment.

##### Reflecting a Polygon on the Coordinate Plane

Reflecting a polygon involves reflecting each of its vertices. Here are the steps:

1. Identify the vertices of the polygon that you want to reflect.
2. Determine the line of reflection.
3. Apply the reflection formulas to each vertex to calculate the new vertices of the reflected polygon.
4. Connect the new vertices to form the reflected polygon.

##### Example

Let's consider an example to illustrate these steps. Suppose we have a triangle with vertices $P$ at $(1, 2)$, $Q$ at $(3, 2)$, and $R$ at $(2, 4)$ and we want to reflect it over the $y$-axis.

Applying the reflection formula to each vertex, we get the reflected points $P'$ at $(-1, 2)$, $Q'$ at $(-3, 2)$, and $R'$ at $(-2, 4)$.

Connecting these points, we get the reflected triangle.

Remember, the process of reflection does not change the distance of the points from the line of reflection. It only changes the position of the points.

### Section: Dilations

#### Subsection: Understanding dilations

Dilations are a type of transformation that alters the size of a figure without changing its shape. They can either enlarge or reduce the figure, depending on the scale factor. The scale factor is a number that determines how much the figure is enlarged or reduced. A scale factor greater than 1 enlarges the figure, while a scale factor less than 1 but greater than 0 reduces the figure. 

##### Performing a Dilation on the Coordinate Plane

Performing a dilation involves multiplying each coordinate of the figure by the scale factor. Here are the steps:

1. Identify the figure that you want to dilate. This could be any figure, but for simplicity, we will focus on polygons.
2. Determine the center of dilation. This is the point that the figure will be dilated from. 
3. Determine the scale factor.
4. Apply the dilation formula to each vertex of the polygon to calculate the new vertices of the dilated polygon.
5. Connect the new vertices to form the dilated polygon.

The dilation formula is:

$$
x' = kx
$$
$$
y' = ky
$$

where $k$ is the scale factor, and $(x', y')$ are the coordinates of the dilated point.

##### Example

Let's consider an example to illustrate these steps. Suppose we have a triangle with vertices $A$ at $(2, 3)$, $B$ at $(5, 3)$, and $C$ at $(4, 6)$ and we want to dilate it by a scale factor of 2 from the origin.

Applying the dilation formula to point $A$, we get:

$$
x' = 2 \cdot 2 = 4
$$
$$
y' = 2 \cdot 3 = 6
$$

So, the dilated point $A'$ is at $(4, 6)$.

Applying the dilation formula to point $B$, we get:

$$
x' = 2 \cdot 5 = 10
$$
$$
y' = 2 \cdot 3 = 6
$$

So, the dilated point $B'$ is at $(10, 6)$.

Applying the dilation formula to point $C$, we get:

$$
x' = 2 \cdot 4 = 8
$$
$$
y' = 2 \cdot 6 = 12
$$

So, the dilated point $C'$ is at $(8, 12)$.

Connecting points $A'$, $B'$, and $C'$, we get the dilated triangle.

#### Subsection: Dilation with a Center of Dilation other than the Origin

In the previous example, we performed a dilation with the origin (0,0) as the center of dilation. However, dilations can also be performed with a center of dilation that is not the origin. In such cases, the dilation formula changes slightly. 

The new dilation formula is:

$$
x' = k(x - h) + h
$$
$$
y' = k(y - k) + k
$$

where $k$ is the scale factor, $(h, k)$ is the center of dilation, and $(x', y')$ are the coordinates of the dilated point.

##### Example

Let's consider an example. Suppose we have a square with vertices $P$ at $(2, 2)$, $Q$ at $(2, 4)$, $R$ at $(4, 4)$, and $S$ at $(4, 2)$ and we want to dilate it by a scale factor of 2 from the center of dilation at $(3, 3)$.

Applying the dilation formula to point $P$, we get:

$$
x' = 2 \cdot (2 - 3) + 3 = 1
$$
$$
y' = 2 \cdot (2 - 3) + 3 = 1
$$

So, the dilated point $P'$ is at $(1, 1)$.

Applying the dilation formula to point $Q$, we get:

$$
x' = 2 \cdot (2 - 3) + 3 = 1
$$
$$
y' = 2 \cdot (4 - 3) + 3 = 5
$$

So, the dilated point $Q'$ is at $(1, 5)$.

Applying the dilation formula to point $R$, we get:

$$
x' = 2 \cdot (4 - 3) + 3 = 5
$$
$$
y' = 2 \cdot (4 - 3) + 3 = 5
$$

So, the dilated point $R'$ is at $(5, 5)$.

Applying the dilation formula to point $S$, we get:

$$
x' = 2 \cdot (4 - 3) + 3 = 5
$$
$$
y' = 2 \cdot (2 - 3) + 3 = 1
$$

So, the dilated point $S'$ is at $(5, 1)$.

Connecting points $P'$, $Q'$, $R'$, and $S'$, we get the dilated square.

As you can see, the process of performing a dilation with a center of dilation other than the origin is similar to the process with the origin as the center of dilation. The only difference is the use of the new dilation formula.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of transformations in Integrated Math 1. We have explored the fundamental concepts of transformations, including translations, rotations, reflections, and dilations. We have learned how these transformations can change the position, size, and orientation of a figure without altering its shape or size. 

We have also discovered how to perform these transformations on the coordinate plane, using algebraic rules and geometric reasoning. We have seen how transformations can be combined to create complex movements and how they can be used to solve real-world problems. 

In addition, we have learned about the properties of transformations, such as the fact that translations, rotations, and reflections are isometries, preserving the distance between points, and that dilations change the size but not the shape of figures. 

We hope that this chapter has provided you with a solid foundation in understanding and performing transformations. As you continue your journey in Integrated Math 1, remember that transformations are not just abstract mathematical concepts, but tools that can help you understand and describe the world around you.

### Exercises

Given a triangle with vertices at $(0,0)$, $(4,0)$, and $(0,3)$, perform a translation of 2 units to the right and 3 units up. What are the coordinates of the vertices of the translated triangle?

#### Exercise 2
Perform a reflection of the point $(5,7)$ across the x-axis. What are the coordinates of the reflected point?

#### Exercise 3
Perform a 90-degree counterclockwise rotation of the point $(2,2)$ about the origin. What are the coordinates of the rotated point?

#### Exercise 4
Perform a dilation of the rectangle with vertices at $(1,1)$, $(1,3)$, $(4,1)$, and $(4,3)$ by a scale factor of 2, with the center of dilation at the origin. What are the coordinates of the vertices of the dilated rectangle?

#### Exercise 5
Given a square with vertices at $(0,0)$, $(0,2)$, $(2,0)$, and $(2,2)$, perform a sequence of transformations: a reflection across the y-axis, followed by a translation 3 units down. What are the coordinates of the vertices of the final figure?

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of transformations in Integrated Math 1. We have explored the fundamental concepts of transformations, including translations, rotations, reflections, and dilations. We have learned how these transformations can change the position, size, and orientation of a figure without altering its shape or size. 

We have also discovered how to perform these transformations on the coordinate plane, using algebraic rules and geometric reasoning. We have seen how transformations can be combined to create complex movements and how they can be used to solve real-world problems. 

In addition, we have learned about the properties of transformations, such as the fact that translations, rotations, and reflections are isometries, preserving the distance between points, and that dilations change the size but not the shape of figures. 

We hope that this chapter has provided you with a solid foundation in understanding and performing transformations. As you continue your journey in Integrated Math 1, remember that transformations are not just abstract mathematical concepts, but tools that can help you understand and describe the world around you.

#### Exercise 3

#### Exercise 4

## Chapter: Transformation properties and proofs

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Transformation properties and proofs. This chapter is designed to provide a comprehensive understanding of the fundamental concepts of transformations in the realm of Integrated Math 1. We will delve into the properties of transformations and how they can be proven mathematically. 

Transformations are a crucial part of mathematics, providing a way to manipulate shapes and functions without altering their fundamental properties. They are the operations that can be performed on a geometric figure to change its position, size, or shape. The four basic types of transformations include translations, reflections, rotations, and dilations. Each of these transformations has unique properties that we will explore in this chapter.

Proofs, on the other hand, are logical arguments that demonstrate the truth of a mathematical statement. They are the backbone of mathematics, providing a rigorous way to establish the validity of mathematical concepts and theorems. In this chapter, we will learn how to construct proofs for the properties of transformations.

This chapter will guide you through the process of understanding the properties of transformations and how to prove them. We will start with the basics and gradually move towards more complex concepts. By the end of this chapter, you should be able to understand and apply the properties of transformations and construct mathematical proofs to validate these properties.

Remember, understanding transformations and proofs is not just about memorizing formulas and theorems. It's about developing a deep understanding of the concepts and learning how to apply them in various mathematical contexts. So, let's embark on this exciting journey of exploring transformation properties and proofs in Integrated Math 1.

## Chapter: Transformation properties and proofs

### Section: Dilation preserved properties

Dilation is one of the four basic types of transformations in mathematics. It involves changing the size of a geometric figure without altering its shape. This transformation can either enlarge or reduce the figure based on a scale factor. The scale factor is a number that determines how much the figure is enlarged or reduced. A scale factor greater than 1 enlarges the figure, while a scale factor less than 1 but greater than 0 reduces it.

#### Understanding properties preserved under dilation

When a geometric figure undergoes dilation, certain properties are preserved. These preserved properties are crucial in understanding the behavior of the figure under dilation. Let's delve into these properties:

1. **Shape**: The shape of the figure remains the same under dilation. This means that the figure will look the same, only its size will change.

2. **Angles**: The measure of the angles in the figure remains the same. This means that if a figure has a right angle before dilation, it will still have a right angle after dilation.

3. **Proportional lengths**: The lengths of the sides of the figure change proportionally. This means that if one side of the figure is twice as long as another side before dilation, it will still be twice as long after dilation.

4. **Collinearity and betweenness**: Points that are collinear (lying on the same line) or between other points before dilation will remain so after dilation.

5. **Parallelism**: Lines that are parallel before dilation will remain parallel after dilation.

Now that we understand the properties preserved under dilation, let's look at how we can prove these properties. Proofs in mathematics provide a logical argument that demonstrates the truth of these properties. They involve a series of logical steps that start from known facts and lead to the property we want to prove.

In the next section, we will delve into the proofs of these properties. We will start with simple proofs and gradually move towards more complex ones. By the end of this section, you should be able to construct mathematical proofs for the properties preserved under dilation.

Remember, understanding dilation and its preserved properties is not just about memorizing these properties. It's about developing a deep understanding of the concepts and learning how to apply them in various mathematical contexts. So, let's continue our journey of exploring transformation properties and proofs in Integrated Math 1.

### Section: Applying properties of dilation

Now that we have a solid understanding of the properties preserved under dilation, let's apply these properties to solve some problems. This will not only reinforce our understanding but also equip us with the necessary skills to tackle similar problems in the future.

#### Example 1: Determining the scale factor

Given a rectangle ABCD and its image A'B'C'D' after dilation, if AB = 4 units, BC = 3 units, A'B' = 8 units, and B'C' = 6 units, what is the scale factor of the dilation?

To solve this problem, we need to remember the property of proportional lengths preserved under dilation. The lengths of the sides of the figure change proportionally. This means that the ratio of the lengths of corresponding sides of the figure and its image after dilation is equal to the scale factor.

So, we can set up the following equation:

$$
\frac{A'B'}{AB} = \frac{B'C'}{BC}
$$

Substituting the given lengths, we get:

$$
\frac{8}{4} = \frac{6}{3}
$$

Solving this equation, we find that the scale factor is 2.

#### Example 2: Verifying preserved properties

Given a triangle XYZ and its image X'Y'Z' after dilation with a scale factor of 3, if XY = 5 units, YZ = 7 units, XZ = 9 units, X'Y' = 15 units, Y'Z' = 21 units, and X'Z' = 27 units, are the properties of shape, angles, proportional lengths, collinearity and betweenness, and parallelism preserved?

To verify these properties, we need to check each one individually:

1. **Shape**: The shape of the figure remains the same under dilation. Since the triangle XYZ is still a triangle after dilation, the shape is preserved.

2. **Angles**: The measure of the angles in the figure remains the same. Without specific angle measures, we can't verify this property directly. However, if we know that XYZ is a right triangle, and X'Y'Z' is also a right triangle, then the angles are preserved.

3. **Proportional lengths**: The lengths of the sides of the figure change proportionally. We can check this by comparing the ratios of the lengths of corresponding sides before and after dilation. If the ratios are equal to the scale factor, then the lengths are proportional. In this case, $\frac{X'Y'}{XY} = \frac{15}{5} = 3$, $\frac{Y'Z'}{YZ} = \frac{21}{7} = 3$, and $\frac{X'Z'}{XZ} = \frac{27}{9} = 3$, which are all equal to the scale factor. Therefore, the lengths are proportional.

4. **Collinearity and betweenness**: Points that are collinear or between other points before dilation will remain so after dilation. Without specific points, we can't verify this property directly. However, if we know that point Y is between points X and Z, and point Y' is between points X' and Z', then the property of betweenness is preserved.

5. **Parallelism**: Lines that are parallel before dilation will remain parallel after dilation. Without specific lines, we can't verify this property directly. However, if we know that line XY is parallel to line X'Y', and line YZ is parallel to line Y'Z', then the property of parallelism is preserved.

In the next section, we will delve into more complex applications of dilation and its preserved properties.

### Section: Properties & definitions of transformations

Transformations are operations that alter the position, orientation, and size of shapes. They are fundamental concepts in geometry and are used to describe and analyze the physical world around us. In this section, we will explore the properties and definitions of transformations, focusing on four main types: translations, reflections, rotations, and dilations.

#### Translation

A translation, also known as a slide, moves every point of a shape the same distance in the same direction. The properties of the shape, such as size, shape, and orientation, remain unchanged. The only thing that changes is the position of the shape.

#### Reflection

A reflection, or flip, is a transformation that uses a line of reflection (mirror line) to create a mirror image of the original shape. The size and shape of the figure remain the same, but the orientation is reversed. If the shape is reflected over the x-axis, the y-coordinates change sign, and if reflected over the y-axis, the x-coordinates change sign.

#### Rotation

A rotation turns a shape about a fixed point, known as the center of rotation. The angle of rotation determines how far the shape is turned. The size, shape, and orientation of the figure remain the same, but the position changes.

#### Dilation

A dilation is a transformation that changes the size of a shape but keeps its shape and orientation. It is characterized by a scale factor, which determines how much the shape is enlarged or reduced. If the scale factor is greater than 1, the shape enlarges; if it's between 0 and 1, the shape reduces.

### Subsection: Understanding transformation properties

Understanding the properties of transformations is crucial for solving problems in geometry. Here are some key properties to remember:

1. **Preservation of shape**: In all transformations except dilation, the shape of the figure remains the same.

2. **Preservation of size**: In translations, reflections, and rotations, the size of the figure remains the same. In dilations, the size changes according to the scale factor.

3. **Preservation of orientation**: In translations and rotations, the orientation of the figure remains the same. In reflections, the orientation is reversed. In dilations, the orientation remains the same.

4. **Preservation of distance**: In translations, reflections, and rotations, the distances between points remain the same. In dilations, distances change according to the scale factor.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into these properties and explore how they can be used to prove geometric theorems and solve problems.

```
and rotations, the size of the figure remains the same.

3. **Preservation of orientation**: In translations and rotations, the orientation of the figure remains the same.

4. **Change of position**: In all transformations, the position of the figure can change.

5. **Scale factor**: In dilations, a scale factor is used to enlarge or reduce the size of the figure.

### Subsection: Applying transformation properties

Now that we understand the properties of transformations, let's apply them to solve problems. 

#### Example 1: Translation

Consider a rectangle with vertices at A(2,3), B(2,5), C(4,5), and D(4,3). Translate the rectangle 3 units to the right and 2 units up. The new coordinates of the rectangle will be A'(5,5), B'(5,7), C'(7,7), and D'(7,5).

#### Example 2: Reflection

Consider a triangle with vertices at P(1,1), Q(3,1), and R(2,3). Reflect the triangle over the x-axis. The new coordinates of the triangle will be P'(-1,1), Q'(-3,1), and R'(-2,3).

#### Example 3: Rotation

Consider a square with vertices at E(1,1), F(1,3), G(3,3), and H(3,1). Rotate the square 90 degrees counterclockwise about the origin. The new coordinates of the square will be E'(-1,1), F'(-3,1), G'(-3,3), and H'(-1,3).

#### Example 4: Dilation

Consider a circle with a radius of 2 units and center at the origin. Dilate the circle by a scale factor of 1.5. The new radius of the circle will be 3 units.

Remember, the key to mastering transformations is understanding their properties and knowing how to apply them. Practice these examples and try creating your own problems to solve. In the next section, we will delve into proofs involving transformations.

### Section: Symmetry

Symmetry is a fundamental concept in mathematics and is closely related to transformations. It is a property where something is unchanged or invariant under some transformation, such as reflection, rotation, or translation. In this section, we will explore the concept of symmetry, its types, and how it relates to transformations.

#### Understanding symmetry

Symmetry is a property that indicates that one shape becomes exactly like another when you move it in some way, such as turning, flipping, or sliding. For example, a square has symmetry because it remains the same after a rotation of 90 degrees.

There are three basic types of symmetry:

1. **Reflection Symmetry (or Line Symmetry)**: A shape has reflection symmetry if there is a line going through it which divides it into two pieces that are mirror images of each other.

2. **Rotational Symmetry**: A shape has rotational symmetry if it can be rotated about a point by an angle less than 360 degrees and still look the same.

3. **Translational Symmetry**: A pattern has translational symmetry if it can be translated (or slid) along a vector and still look the same.

Let's look at each of these in more detail.

#### Reflection Symmetry

Reflection symmetry, also known as line symmetry, is when one half of a figure is the mirror image of the other half. The line of symmetry is the imaginary line where you could fold the image and have both halves match exactly. 

For example, consider a rectangle. It has two lines of symmetry - one that cuts it into two equal halves horizontally, and another that cuts it vertically. 

#### Rotational Symmetry

Rotational symmetry, also known as radial symmetry, is when an object looks the same after a certain amount of rotation. An object has rotational symmetry if its appearance does not change after rotation less than a full turn. 

For example, a square has rotational symmetry. If you turn it 90 degrees, it still looks the same. 

#### Translational Symmetry

Translational symmetry is when an object can be moved (or translated) without changing its appearance. This is often seen in patterns or tessellations. 

For example, consider a pattern of evenly spaced dots. If you slide the pattern to the right or left, it still looks the same. 

Understanding symmetry can help us understand and solve problems involving transformations. In the next section, we will explore how symmetry is used in mathematical proofs.

#### Translational Symmetry

Translational symmetry, also known as slide symmetry, is when a pattern can be slid or translated along a vector and still look the same. This type of symmetry is often seen in patterns and tessellations. 

For example, consider a pattern of evenly spaced dots in a line. If you slide the pattern along the line, it still looks the same. This is because the pattern repeats itself at regular intervals.

### Section: Identifying symmetry in shapes and graphs

Now that we have a basic understanding of the different types of symmetry, let's explore how to identify symmetry in shapes and graphs. 

#### Identifying Reflection Symmetry

To identify reflection symmetry in a shape, look for a line that you can draw through the shape such that the shape on one side of the line is a mirror image of the shape on the other side. This line is called the line of symmetry.

For example, an equilateral triangle has 3 lines of symmetry, each passing through a vertex and the midpoint of the opposite side.

In graphs, reflection symmetry can be identified around the y-axis, x-axis, or the origin. A graph has symmetry around the y-axis if, for every point $(x, y)$ on the graph, the point $(-x, y)$ is also on the graph. Similarly, a graph has symmetry around the x-axis if, for every point $(x, y)$ on the graph, the point $(x, -y)$ is also on the graph. Lastly, a graph has symmetry around the origin if, for every point $(x, y)$ on the graph, the point $(-x, -y)$ is also on the graph.

#### Identifying Rotational Symmetry

To identify rotational symmetry in a shape, try rotating the shape about a point. If the shape looks the same in more than one position, then it has rotational symmetry. The number of positions in which the shape looks the same is called the order of symmetry.

For example, a square has rotational symmetry of order 4, because it looks the same in 4 positions (0 degrees, 90 degrees, 180 degrees, and 270 degrees).

In graphs, rotational symmetry can be identified around the origin. A graph has rotational symmetry around the origin if, for every point $(x, y)$ on the graph, the point $(-x, -y)$ is also on the graph.

#### Identifying Translational Symmetry

To identify translational symmetry in a pattern, look for a vector along which you can slide the pattern and it still looks the same. 

For example, a pattern of evenly spaced dots in a line has translational symmetry, because you can slide the pattern along the line and it still looks the same.

In graphs, translational symmetry is less common, but can be seen in periodic functions like sine and cosine, where the graph repeats itself at regular intervals.

In the next section, we will explore how to use these concepts of symmetry to solve mathematical problems and proofs.

### Section: Proofs with transformations

In this section, we will delve into the world of geometric proofs, specifically focusing on proofs involving transformations. Transformations are operations that alter the position, orientation, or size of a shape. The four main types of transformations are translations, reflections, rotations, and dilations. 

#### Understanding geometric proofs

Geometric proofs are logical arguments that use axioms, theorems, and definitions to demonstrate the truth of a geometric statement. They are a fundamental part of geometry and are used to establish the validity of geometric concepts and relationships. 

In the context of transformations, geometric proofs can be used to show that a certain transformation has occurred or to prove properties about the transformation. For example, you might use a geometric proof to show that a shape has been reflected over a line or to prove that a rotation preserves the size of a shape.

Let's consider a simple example. Suppose we have a rectangle ABCD and we want to prove that after a 90-degree clockwise rotation about the origin, rectangle ABCD coincides with rectangle A'B'C'D'.

We can start by stating what we know:

1. Rectangle ABCD has vertices A(2,3), B(5,3), C(5,1), and D(2,1).
2. The rule for a 90-degree clockwise rotation about the origin is $(x, y) \rightarrow (y, -x)$.

Applying this rule to each vertex of the rectangle, we get:

1. A'(3, -2)
2. B'(3, -5)
3. C'(1, -5)
4. D'(1, -2)

These are the vertices of rectangle A'B'C'D'. Therefore, we have shown that after a 90-degree clockwise rotation about the origin, rectangle ABCD coincides with rectangle A'B'C'D'.

This is a simple example of a geometric proof involving transformations. As you continue to study Integrated Math 1, you will encounter more complex proofs and learn more about the fascinating world of geometry.

### Section: Constructing proofs with transformations

In the previous section, we learned about the basics of geometric proofs involving transformations. Now, let's delve deeper into how to construct these proofs. 

#### Understanding the transformation

The first step in constructing a proof with transformations is understanding the transformation itself. This involves identifying the type of transformation (translation, reflection, rotation, or dilation) and the properties associated with it. For example, a reflection changes the orientation of a shape but not its size, while a dilation changes the size but not the orientation.

#### Identifying the given and the to-prove

Next, identify what is given and what needs to be proved. The given is the information that you start with, such as the coordinates of the vertices of a shape or the rule for a transformation. The to-prove is the statement that you are trying to demonstrate. This could be a property of the transformation or a statement about the result of the transformation.

#### Constructing the proof

Now, it's time to construct the proof. Start by stating the given information. Then, apply the transformation to the given shape, using the rule for the transformation. This will give you the coordinates of the vertices of the transformed shape.

Next, use the properties of the transformation to make deductions about the transformed shape. For example, if the transformation is a reflection, you might use the property that a reflection changes the orientation but not the size of a shape to deduce that the transformed shape has the same size as the original shape.

Finally, compare the transformed shape with the to-prove statement. If they match, then you have successfully constructed a proof with transformations.

#### Example

Let's consider an example. Suppose we have a triangle ABC with vertices A(1,2), B(3,2), and C(2,4). We are given that triangle ABC is reflected over the x-axis to form triangle A'B'C'. We want to prove that triangle ABC is congruent to triangle A'B'C'.

Here's how we can construct the proof:

1. Given: Triangle ABC with vertices A(1,2), B(3,2), and C(2,4). The rule for a reflection over the x-axis is $(x, y) \rightarrow (x, -y)$.
2. Apply the transformation: A'(1,-2), B'(3,-2), C'(2,-4).
3. Use the properties of the reflection: A reflection changes the orientation but not the size of a shape. Therefore, triangle ABC has the same size as triangle A'B'C'.
4. Compare with the to-prove statement: Triangle ABC is congruent to triangle A'B'C'. 

Therefore, we have successfully proved that triangle ABC is congruent to triangle A'B'C' after a reflection over the x-axis.

As you can see, constructing proofs with transformations involves a combination of understanding the transformation, identifying the given and the to-prove, and using logical reasoning to demonstrate the truth of the to-prove statement. With practice, you will become more comfortable with this process and be able to tackle more complex proofs.
```
