```
#### Exercise 4
Given a triangle with vertices at $(0,0)$, $(3,0)$, and $(0,2)$, find the coordinates of the triangle after a dilation with a scale factor of 2 about the origin.

#### Exercise 5
Prove that the distance between two points is invariant under translation. That is, if a point $(x_1, y_1)$ is translated to $(x_1 + a, y_1 + b)$ and a point $(x_2, y_2)$ is translated to $(x_2 + a, y_2 + b)$, the distance between the two points before the translation is the same as the distance between the two points after the translation.

## Chapter: Congruence

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Congruence, a fundamental concept in the world of Integrated Math 1. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the concept of congruence, its principles, and its applications in various mathematical problems.

Congruence, in mathematics, is a term used to describe the equality of shapes and sizes of two or more objects. It is a relation that holds between two things that have the same size and shape. In the realm of geometry, two figures or objects are congruent if they have the same shape and size, or if one has the same shape and size as the mirror image of the other.

In this chapter, we will delve into the concept of congruence, starting from its basic definition, to its properties and theorems. We will explore how congruence is determined and measured, and how it is used in solving mathematical problems. We will also look at the different types of congruence, such as congruence in triangles, circles, and other geometric shapes.

We will also discuss the concept of congruence in the context of number theory, where it is used to describe the relationship between integers that have the same remainder when divided by a certain number. This is often represented as `$a \equiv b \mod m$`, where `$a$` and `$b$` are the integers, and `$m$` is the modulus.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the concept of congruence, its principles, and its applications in various mathematical problems. You should be able to identify congruent figures, apply the properties of congruence in solving problems, and understand the role of congruence in number theory.

Remember, mastering the concept of congruence is not just about memorizing definitions and theorems, but about understanding how these principles work and how they can be applied in solving real-world problems. So, let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of congruence in Integrated Math 1.

## Chapter: Congruence

### Section: Triangle congruence from transformations

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of congruence and explored its various applications in geometry and number theory. Now, we will delve deeper into the concept of congruence in triangles and understand how transformations can lead to congruent triangles.

#### Understanding triangle congruence

Triangles are among the simplest geometric shapes, yet they hold a wealth of mathematical properties. One of these properties is congruence. Two triangles are congruent if they have the same size and shape. This means that their corresponding sides are equal in length, and their corresponding angles are equal in measure.

In Integrated Math 1, we often use transformations to determine if two triangles are congruent. A transformation is a process that changes the position, size, or shape of a figure. There are four basic types of transformations: translations, rotations, reflections, and dilations.

- **Translation**: A translation "slides" a figure along a straight path in a certain direction. If a triangle is translated, its size and shape remain unchanged. Therefore, a translated triangle is congruent to the original triangle.

- **Rotation**: A rotation "turns" a figure around a fixed point, called the center of rotation. If a triangle is rotated, its size and shape remain unchanged. Therefore, a rotated triangle is congruent to the original triangle.

- **Reflection**: A reflection "flips" a figure over a line, called the line of reflection. If a triangle is reflected, its size and shape remain unchanged. Therefore, a reflected triangle is congruent to the original triangle.

- **Dilation**: A dilation "stretches" or "shrinks" a figure from a fixed point, called the center of dilation. If a triangle is dilated, its size changes, but its shape remains the same. Therefore, a dilated triangle is not necessarily congruent to the original triangle, unless the scale factor of the dilation is 1.

In the next sections, we will explore each of these transformations in detail and learn how to use them to determine triangle congruence. We will also introduce the concept of similarity, which is closely related to congruence and transformations. By the end of this section, you should be able to use transformations to determine if two triangles are congruent, and understand the relationship between congruence, similarity, and transformations.

#### Proving triangle congruence with transformations

In this section, we will explore how to use transformations to prove that two triangles are congruent. This is a powerful technique that can simplify many problems in geometry.

##### Using translations to prove congruence

If we can translate one triangle so that it exactly overlaps another triangle, then the two triangles are congruent. This is because a translation does not change the size or shape of a figure. 

For example, consider two triangles $ABC$ and $DEF$. If we can find a translation that moves triangle $ABC$ to the position of triangle $DEF$, then $ABC$ is congruent to $DEF$.

##### Using rotations to prove congruence

Similarly, if we can rotate one triangle around a point so that it exactly overlaps another triangle, then the two triangles are congruent. This is because a rotation does not change the size or shape of a figure.

For example, consider two triangles $ABC$ and $DEF$. If we can find a rotation that turns triangle $ABC$ to the position of triangle $DEF$, then $ABC$ is congruent to $DEF$.

##### Using reflections to prove congruence

If we can reflect one triangle over a line so that it exactly overlaps another triangle, then the two triangles are congruent. This is because a reflection does not change the size or shape of a figure.

For example, consider two triangles $ABC$ and $DEF$. If we can find a reflection that flips triangle $ABC$ to the position of triangle $DEF$, then $ABC$ is congruent to $DEF$.

##### Using dilations to prove congruence

Dilations are a bit different from the other transformations. A dilation changes the size of a figure, but not its shape. Therefore, if we can dilate one triangle so that it exactly overlaps another triangle, then the two triangles are similar, but not necessarily congruent. 

For example, consider two triangles $ABC$ and $DEF$. If we can find a dilation that resizes triangle $ABC$ to the size of triangle $DEF$, then $ABC$ is similar to $DEF$. However, if the scale factor of the dilation is 1 (meaning the size of the triangle does not change), then $ABC$ is congruent to $DEF$.

In conclusion, transformations provide a powerful tool for proving triangle congruence. By understanding how translations, rotations, reflections, and dilations affect a figure, we can determine whether two triangles are congruent.

### Section: Congruent triangles

#### Identifying congruent triangles

In the previous section, we learned how to prove the congruence of two triangles using transformations. Now, we will focus on identifying congruent triangles without the need for transformations. 

##### Congruent triangles and corresponding parts

Two triangles are congruent if they have the same size and shape. This means that their corresponding sides are equal in length, and their corresponding angles are equal in measure. 

For example, consider two triangles $ABC$ and $DEF$. If $AB = DE$, $BC = EF$, and $AC = DF$ (corresponding sides are equal), and $\angle A = \angle D$, $\angle B = \angle E$, and $\angle C = \angle F$ (corresponding angles are equal), then triangle $ABC$ is congruent to triangle $DEF$.

##### Congruence postulates

There are several postulates that we can use to prove the congruence of two triangles without having to show that all corresponding sides and angles are equal. These postulates are based on the properties of triangles and the definition of congruence.

###### Side-Side-Side (SSS) Postulate

If the three sides of one triangle are congruent to the three sides of another triangle, then the two triangles are congruent. 

###### Side-Angle-Side (SAS) Postulate

If two sides and the included angle of one triangle are congruent to two sides and the included angle of another triangle, then the two triangles are congruent.

###### Angle-Side-Angle (ASA) Postulate

If two angles and the included side of one triangle are congruent to two angles and the included side of another triangle, then the two triangles are congruent.

###### Angle-Angle-Side (AAS) Postulate

If two angles and a non-included side of one triangle are congruent to two angles and a non-included side of another triangle, then the two triangles are congruent.

###### Hypotenuse-Leg (HL) Postulate

This postulate applies only to right triangles. If the hypotenuse and one leg of a right triangle are congruent to the hypotenuse and one leg of another right triangle, then the two triangles are congruent.

In the following sections, we will explore each of these postulates in detail and learn how to use them to prove triangle congruence.

```
#### Hypotenuse-Leg (HL) Postulate (Continued)

This postulate applies only to right triangles. If the hypotenuse and one leg of a right triangle are congruent to the hypotenuse and one leg of another right triangle, then the two triangles are congruent.

### Section: Applying properties of congruent triangles

Now that we have learned how to identify congruent triangles, let's apply these properties to solve problems. 

#### Using congruent triangles in proofs

Congruent triangles are often used in geometric proofs. By proving that two triangles are congruent, we can conclude that their corresponding parts are also congruent. This can be a powerful tool in solving complex geometric problems.

For example, consider a problem where we are given that triangle $ABC$ is congruent to triangle $DEF$, and we are asked to prove that $\angle BAC = \angle EDF$. Since the triangles are congruent, we know that their corresponding angles are equal. Therefore, $\angle BAC = \angle EDF$.

#### Solving problems with congruent triangles

In addition to proofs, congruent triangles can also be used to solve problems. For example, if we know that two triangles are congruent, we can use the properties of congruent triangles to find unknown side lengths or angle measures.

Consider a problem where we are given that triangle $ABC$ is congruent to triangle $DEF$, and we are asked to find the length of $DE$. If we know the length of $AB$, we can conclude that $DE = AB$ because corresponding sides of congruent triangles are equal.

In conclusion, congruent triangles are a fundamental concept in geometry. By understanding how to identify and apply the properties of congruent triangles, you can solve a wide range of geometric problems. In the next section, we will explore similar triangles, which are another important concept in geometry.

### Section: Working with triangles

Triangles are one of the most fundamental shapes in geometry. They are simple, yet they possess a rich set of properties that make them incredibly useful in solving a wide range of problems. In this section, we will explore some of these properties and learn how to work with triangles effectively.

#### Understanding triangle properties

Triangles have several important properties that are directly related to their sides and angles. Understanding these properties is crucial for mastering the concept of congruence in triangles.

##### Property 1: Sum of angles

The sum of the interior angles of a triangle is always $180^\circ$. This is true regardless of the shape or size of the triangle. This property can be used to find the measure of an unknown angle in a triangle if the measures of the other two angles are known.

##### Property 2: Congruence of sides and angles

In a triangle, the largest angle is opposite the longest side, and the smallest angle is opposite the shortest side. Conversely, the longest side is opposite the largest angle, and the shortest side is opposite the smallest angle. This property is particularly useful when working with congruent triangles, as it allows us to make conclusions about the relative lengths of sides and measures of angles.

##### Property 3: Pythagorean theorem

The Pythagorean theorem is a fundamental property of right triangles. It states that in a right triangle, the square of the length of the hypotenuse (the side opposite the right angle) is equal to the sum of the squares of the lengths of the other two sides. This theorem can be used to find the length of a side in a right triangle if the lengths of the other two sides are known.

##### Property 4: Triangle inequality theorem

The triangle inequality theorem states that the sum of the lengths of any two sides of a triangle is always greater than the length of the third side. This theorem is useful for determining whether a given set of three lengths can form a triangle.

In the next section, we will apply these properties to solve problems involving triangles. By mastering these properties, you will be well-equipped to tackle a wide range of geometric problems.

### Section: Applying triangle properties

Now that we have a good understanding of the basic properties of triangles, let's apply these properties to solve problems involving triangles. This will not only help us to reinforce our understanding of these properties but also develop our problem-solving skills.

#### Subsection: Applying the sum of angles property

Let's start with the sum of angles property. Suppose we have a triangle with two known angles, say $50^\circ$ and $70^\circ$. We can use the sum of angles property to find the measure of the third angle. Since the sum of the angles in a triangle is $180^\circ$, the third angle is $180^\circ - 50^\circ - 70^\circ = 60^\circ$.

#### Subsection: Applying the congruence of sides and angles property

Next, let's consider the congruence of sides and angles property. Suppose we have a triangle with sides of lengths 5, 7, and 9 units. The longest side is 9 units, so the largest angle is opposite this side. Similarly, the shortest side is 5 units, so the smallest angle is opposite this side. This property can help us determine the relative measures of the angles in the triangle.

#### Subsection: Applying the Pythagorean theorem

The Pythagorean theorem is a powerful tool for solving problems involving right triangles. For example, suppose we have a right triangle with sides of lengths 3 and 4 units. We can use the Pythagorean theorem to find the length of the hypotenuse. According to the theorem, the square of the hypotenuse is equal to the sum of the squares of the other two sides, so the hypotenuse is $\sqrt{3^2 + 4^2} = 5$ units.

#### Subsection: Applying the triangle inequality theorem

Finally, let's apply the triangle inequality theorem. Suppose we have three line segments with lengths 4, 5, and 9 units. We can use the triangle inequality theorem to determine whether these line segments can form a triangle. According to the theorem, the sum of the lengths of any two sides must be greater than the length of the third side. In this case, 4 + 5 = 9, which is not greater than 9, so these line segments cannot form a triangle.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of congruence and explore how we can use these properties to prove that two triangles are congruent.

### Section: Theorems concerning quadrilateral properties

Quadrilaterals, like triangles, have their own unique properties that can be used to solve problems. In this section, we will explore some of the key theorems concerning quadrilateral properties.

#### Subsection: Understanding quadrilateral properties

A quadrilateral is a polygon with four sides. There are several types of quadrilaterals, including squares, rectangles, parallelograms, rhombuses, and trapezoids. Each of these has its own unique properties, but there are also some properties that are common to all quadrilaterals.

##### The sum of interior angles

The first property is the sum of the interior angles of a quadrilateral. No matter what type of quadrilateral you have, the sum of its interior angles is always $360^\circ$. This can be proven by dividing the quadrilateral into two triangles. Since the sum of the angles in each triangle is $180^\circ$, the sum of the angles in the quadrilateral is $180^\circ \times 2 = 360^\circ$.

##### The opposite sides and angles

In some quadrilaterals, the opposite sides are congruent (equal in length), and the opposite angles are also congruent. This is true for rectangles, squares, and parallelograms. In a rectangle or square, the angles are all right angles ($90^\circ$), while in a parallelogram, the opposite angles are equal, but not necessarily right angles.

##### The diagonals

The properties of the diagonals (lines connecting opposite corners) also vary depending on the type of quadrilateral. In a rectangle or square, the diagonals are congruent and bisect each other (divide each other into two equal parts). In a parallelogram, the diagonals bisect each other, but are not necessarily congruent. In a rhombus, the diagonals are perpendicular (form a right angle) and bisect each other, but are not necessarily congruent.

Understanding these properties can help us solve problems involving quadrilaterals. In the next subsections, we will apply these properties to solve some problems.

#### Subsection: Applying quadrilateral properties

Now that we have a solid understanding of the properties of quadrilaterals, let's apply these properties to solve some problems. 

##### Example 1: Finding missing angles

Suppose we have a quadrilateral with three known angles: $80^\circ$, $100^\circ$, and $90^\circ$. We can find the fourth angle by using the property that the sum of the interior angles of a quadrilateral is $360^\circ$. 

Let's denote the unknown angle as $x$. We can set up the equation:

$$80^\circ + 100^\circ + 90^\circ + x = 360^\circ$$

Solving for $x$, we get:

$$x = 360^\circ - 80^\circ - 100^\circ - 90^\circ = 90^\circ$$

So, the fourth angle is $90^\circ$.

##### Example 2: Identifying quadrilaterals

Suppose we have a quadrilateral with the following properties: all sides are equal in length, and the diagonals are perpendicular and bisect each other. Which type of quadrilateral is this?

From our understanding of quadrilateral properties, we know that a quadrilateral with all sides equal is either a square or a rhombus. However, only a rhombus has diagonals that are perpendicular and bisect each other. Therefore, the quadrilateral is a rhombus.

##### Example 3: Finding side lengths

Suppose we have a rectangle with a diagonal of length 13 units and one side of length 5 units. What is the length of the other side?

In a rectangle, the diagonal divides the rectangle into two right triangles. We can use the Pythagorean theorem, which states that in a right triangle, the square of the length of the hypotenuse (the side opposite the right angle) is equal to the sum of the squares of the lengths of the other two sides. 

Let's denote the unknown side as $a$. We can set up the equation:

$$a^2 + 5^2 = 13^2$$

Solving for $a$, we get:

$$a = \sqrt{13^2 - 5^2} = 12$$

So, the length of the other side is 12 units.

These examples illustrate how understanding and applying the properties of quadrilaterals can help us solve a variety of problems. As you continue to study and practice, you'll become more comfortable with these concepts and be able to apply them in more complex situations.

## Chapter: Congruence

### Section: Proofs of general theorems that use triangle congruence

#### Subsection: Understanding geometric proofs

In this section, we will delve into the world of geometric proofs, specifically focusing on triangle congruence. A geometric proof is a method of determining the validity of a geometric proposition by using logical reasoning and established mathematical principles. 

#### Subsection: Triangle Congruence

Triangles are congruent if they have the same size and shape. This means that their corresponding sides are equal in length, and their corresponding angles are equal in measure. There are several ways to prove that two triangles are congruent:

1. **Side-Side-Side (SSS) Congruence**: If the three sides of one triangle are congruent to the three sides of another triangle, then the triangles are congruent.

2. **Side-Angle-Side (SAS) Congruence**: If two sides and the included angle of one triangle are congruent to two sides and the included angle of another triangle, then the triangles are congruent.

3. **Angle-Side-Angle (ASA) Congruence**: If two angles and the included side of one triangle are congruent to two angles and the included side of another triangle, then the triangles are congruent.

4. **Angle-Angle-Side (AAS) Congruence**: If two angles and a non-included side of one triangle are congruent to two angles and a non-included side of another triangle, then the triangles are congruent.

Let's look at some examples of how these principles can be applied in geometric proofs.

##### Example 1: Proving Triangle Congruence using SSS

Suppose we have two triangles, $\triangle ABC$ and $\triangle DEF$, with side lengths as follows: $AB = DE = 5$ units, $BC = EF = 7$ units, and $AC = DF = 6$ units. Can we say that $\triangle ABC$ is congruent to $\triangle DEF$?

According to the SSS congruence rule, if the three sides of one triangle are congruent to the three sides of another triangle, then the triangles are congruent. In this case, we can see that $AB = DE$, $BC = EF$, and $AC = DF$. Therefore, $\triangle ABC$ is congruent to $\triangle DEF$ by SSS congruence.

##### Example 2: Proving Triangle Congruence using SAS

Suppose we have two triangles, $\triangle PQR$ and $\triangle STU$, with $PQ = ST = 4$ units, $QR = TU = 6$ units, and $\angle PQR = \angle STU = 60^\circ$. Can we say that $\triangle PQR$ is congruent to $\triangle STU$?

According to the SAS congruence rule, if two sides and the included angle of one triangle are congruent to two sides and the included angle of another triangle, then the triangles are congruent. In this case, we can see that $PQ = ST$, $QR = TU$, and $\angle PQR = \angle STU$. Therefore, $\triangle PQR$ is congruent to $\triangle STU$ by SAS congruence.

Understanding and applying these principles of triangle congruence can help us solve a variety of problems in geometry. In the next section, we will explore more complex geometric proofs that involve congruent triangles.

```
this case, we can see that $AB = DE$, $BC = EF$, and $AC = DF$. Therefore, we can conclude that $\triangle ABC$ is congruent to $\triangle DEF$ by the SSS congruence rule.

##### Example 2: Proving Triangle Congruence using SAS

Consider two triangles, $\triangle PQR$ and $\triangle STU$, with the following measurements: $PQ = ST = 4$ units, $QR = TU = 6$ units, and $\angle PQR = \angle STU = 60^\circ$. Can we say that $\triangle PQR$ is congruent to $\triangle STU$?

According to the SAS congruence rule, if two sides and the included angle of one triangle are congruent to two sides and the included angle of another triangle, then the triangles are congruent. In this case, we can see that $PQ = ST$, $QR = TU$, and $\angle PQR = \angle STU$. Therefore, we can conclude that $\triangle PQR$ is congruent to $\triangle STU$ by the SAS congruence rule.

### Section: Proofs of general theorems that use triangle congruence

#### Subsection: Constructing proofs using triangle congruence

In this subsection, we will learn how to construct proofs using triangle congruence. This involves using the principles of triangle congruence to prove more complex geometric theorems.

##### Example 3: Proving the Isosceles Triangle Theorem

The Isosceles Triangle Theorem states that if two sides of a triangle are congruent, then the angles opposite those sides are congruent. Let's prove this theorem using triangle congruence.

Consider an isosceles triangle $\triangle XYZ$ with $XY = XZ$. We draw a line from $X$ to $YZ$ at point $W$ such that $YW = WZ$. Now, we have two triangles, $\triangle XYW$ and $\triangle XZW$.

We can see that $XY = XZ$ (given), $XW = XW$ (reflexive property), and $YW = WZ$ (construction). Therefore, by the SSS congruence rule, $\triangle XYW$ is congruent to $\triangle XZW$.

Since corresponding parts of congruent triangles are congruent (CPCTC), we can say that $\angle Y = \angle Z$. Therefore, we have proved the Isosceles Triangle Theorem using triangle congruence.

In the next section, we will continue to explore more complex proofs using triangle congruence. Remember, practice is key to mastering these concepts, so be sure to work through plenty of examples on your own.
```

## Chapter: Congruence

### Section: Constructing lines & angles

#### Subsection: Understanding lines and angles

In this section, we will delve into the basics of lines and angles, which are fundamental concepts in geometry. Understanding these concepts is crucial for mastering the principles of congruence.

##### Lines

In geometry, a line is a straight one-dimensional figure that extends infinitely in both directions. It is often described as the shortest distance between any two points. Here are some important terms related to lines:

- **Line Segment**: A part of a line that is bounded by two distinct end points, and contains every point on the line between its endpoints.
- **Ray**: A part of the line that starts at a certain point and goes off in a certain direction to infinity.
- **Parallel Lines**: Two lines in a plane that do not intersect or touch each other at any point are called parallel lines.
- **Perpendicular Lines**: Two lines that intersect at right angles are called perpendicular lines.

##### Angles

An angle measures the amount of turn between two lines that meet at a point, or vertex. The units used to measure angles are degrees (°) or radians (rad). Here are some important terms related to angles:

- **Acute Angle**: An angle that is less than 90°.
- **Right Angle**: An angle that is exactly 90°.
- **Obtuse Angle**: An angle that is greater than 90° but less than 180°.
- **Straight Angle**: An angle of 180° is a straight angle, which forms a straight line.
- **Reflex Angle**: An angle that is greater than 180° but less than 360°.

Understanding these basic concepts will help us in the construction of lines and angles, and in proving congruence between different geometric figures. In the next section, we will learn how to construct lines and angles using a compass and straightedge.

### Section: Constructing lines & angles

#### Subsection: Constructing lines and angles with a compass and straightedge

In this section, we will learn how to construct lines and angles using a compass and straightedge. This is a fundamental skill in geometry and is often used in proofs and problem-solving.

##### Constructing a Line Segment

To construct a line segment of a given length:

1. Draw a point on your paper. This will be one end of your line segment.
2. Place the point of your compass on the point you just drew, and adjust the width of your compass to the desired length of your line segment.
3. Draw an arc with your compass. The arc should be long enough to cross the line where you want the other end of your line segment to be.
4. Draw a point where the arc crosses the line. This is the other end of your line segment.
5. Draw a straight line between the two points with your straightedge. This is your line segment.

##### Constructing an Angle

To construct an angle of a given measure:

1. Draw a line segment. This will be one side of your angle.
2. Place the point of your compass on one end of the line segment, and draw an arc that intersects the line segment.
3. Without changing the width of your compass, place the point of your compass on the point where the arc intersects the line segment, and draw another arc that intersects the first arc.
4. Draw a line from the end of the line segment through the point where the arcs intersect. This is the other side of your angle.

Remember, the measure of an angle is the amount of turn between the two lines, measured in degrees or radians. You can use a protractor to measure the angle you've constructed and adjust as necessary.

##### Constructing Parallel and Perpendicular Lines

To construct a line parallel to a given line through a point not on the line:

1. Draw a line segment through the given point that intersects the given line.
2. Construct an angle at the point of intersection that is equal to the angle between the given line and the line segment. The line through the given point and the point of intersection is parallel to the given line.

To construct a line perpendicular to a given line through a point on the line:

1. Draw a line segment from the given point that is not parallel to the given line.
2. Construct an angle at the given point that is 90°. The line through the given point and the point of intersection is perpendicular to the given line.

These constructions are fundamental to many geometric proofs and problems. With practice, you will be able to construct lines and angles quickly and accurately.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of congruence in depth. We have learned that congruence is a fundamental concept in geometry, and it plays a crucial role in understanding and solving various mathematical problems. We have seen how congruence is used to determine whether two figures are identical in shape and size, and how it can be applied to various geometric figures such as triangles, rectangles, and circles.

We have also delved into the properties of congruent figures, and how these properties can be used to solve problems. We have learned that congruent figures have the same size and shape, and that they can be transformed into each other through rotations, translations, and reflections. We have also seen how congruence can be used to prove theorems and postulates in geometry.

In addition, we have explored the concept of congruence in relation to other mathematical concepts such as similarity and equivalence. We have seen how congruence is a special case of similarity, where the ratio of corresponding sides is 1:1, and how it is related to the concept of equivalence in algebra.

Overall, the concept of congruence is a powerful tool in mathematics, and mastering it is essential for success in Integrated Math 1 and beyond.

## Chapter: Analytic Geometry

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Analytic Geometry, a fundamental branch of mathematics that is integral to the study of Integrated Math 1. This chapter will delve into the fascinating world of geometric shapes and figures, their properties, and how they can be represented and analyzed using algebraic methods. 

Analytic Geometry, also known as coordinate geometry or Cartesian geometry, is a branch of mathematics that utilizes algebraic equations to describe geometric properties. It is a bridge that connects the abstract world of geometry with the more concrete realm of algebra. This chapter will provide you with the tools and understanding necessary to navigate this bridge with ease.

In this chapter, we will explore the Cartesian coordinate system, named after the French mathematician René Descartes. This system allows us to represent geometric figures such as points, lines, and curves in a two-dimensional plane using pairs of numerical coordinates. We will learn how to plot points, draw lines, and represent curves using this system.

We will also delve into the equations of lines and circles, two of the most fundamental shapes in Analytic Geometry. You will learn how to derive these equations from geometric properties and how to interpret them to gain insights about the shapes they represent.

Finally, we will explore the concept of distance and its importance in Analytic Geometry. We will learn how to calculate the distance between two points, the length of a line segment, and the radius of a circle using algebraic methods.

This chapter will provide a solid foundation in Analytic Geometry, equipping you with the knowledge and skills to tackle more complex mathematical problems in future studies. So, let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of Analytic Geometry.

## Chapter: Analytic Geometry

### Section: Dividing Line Segments

#### Understanding Line Segments

A line segment is a part of a line that is bounded by two distinct end points, and contains every point on the line between its endpoints. A closed line segment includes both endpoints, while an open line segment excludes both endpoints; a half-open line segment includes exactly one of the endpoints. 

In this section, we will delve into the concept of dividing line segments. This is a fundamental concept in Analytic Geometry and has wide-ranging applications in various fields of mathematics and science.

#### Dividing Line Segments in a Ratio

One of the most common tasks in Analytic Geometry is dividing a line segment in a given ratio. This involves finding a point on the line segment that divides it into two parts, such that the ratio of the lengths of these two parts is equal to the given ratio.

Let's consider a line segment AB with endpoints A(x1, y1) and B(x2, y2). We want to find a point P(x, y) on the line segment AB that divides it in the ratio m:n.

The coordinates (x, y) of the point P can be found using the section formula:

$$
x = \frac{mx2 + nx1}{m + n}
$$

$$
y = \frac{my2 + ny1}{m + n}
$$

This formula gives us the coordinates of the point that divides the line segment AB in the ratio m:n. If the ratio is 1:1, the point P is the midpoint of the line segment.

#### Midpoint of a Line Segment

The midpoint of a line segment is the point that divides the line segment into two equal parts. The coordinates of the midpoint M(x, y) of a line segment with endpoints A(x1, y1) and B(x2, y2) can be found using the midpoint formula:

$$
x = \frac{x1 + x2}{2}
$$

$$
y = \frac{y1 + y2}{2}
$$

This formula gives us the coordinates of the midpoint of the line segment AB.

In the next section, we will explore more complex applications of these concepts, including the division of line segments in three-dimensional space. But for now, practice dividing line segments using the section and midpoint formulas. Remember, the best way to master Analytic Geometry is through consistent practice and application of the concepts.

### Section: Dividing Line Segments

#### Dividing Line Segments into Equal Parts

In the previous sections, we learned how to divide a line segment into two parts in a given ratio, and how to find the midpoint of a line segment, which divides it into two equal parts. Now, let's extend these concepts to dividing a line segment into more than two equal parts.

Consider a line segment AB with endpoints A(x1, y1) and B(x2, y2). We want to divide this line segment into 'n' equal parts. 

To do this, we first need to find the length of the line segment AB. The length (d) of a line segment with endpoints (x1, y1) and (x2, y2) can be found using the distance formula:

$$
d = \sqrt{(x2 - x1)^2 + (y2 - y1)^2}
$$

Once we have the length of the line segment, we can find the length of each part by dividing the total length by the number of parts. Let's denote the length of each part as 'p'. So, 

$$
p = \frac{d}{n}
$$

Now, to find the coordinates of the points that divide the line segment into 'n' equal parts, we can use the concept of vectors. A vector from point A to point B can be represented as (x2 - x1, y2 - y1). 

Let's denote the vector from A to B as AB. Then, the vector from A to each point that divides the line segment into 'n' equal parts can be represented as `n` times the vector AB divided by 'n'. 

So, if P is a point that divides the line segment into 'n' equal parts, the coordinates (x, y) of P can be found as:

$$
x = x1 + \frac{(x2 - x1) * i}{n}
$$

$$
y = y1 + \frac{(y2 - y1) * i}{n}
$$

where 'i' is the number of the part starting from 1 to 'n-1'. 

This method allows us to divide a line segment into any number of equal parts. Practice this method with different line segments and numbers of parts to get a good understanding of it. In the next section, we will explore how to divide line segments in three-dimensional space.

### Section: Problem solving with distance on the coordinate plane

#### Understanding the distance formula

In the previous section, we used the distance formula to find the length of a line segment. Now, let's delve deeper into understanding this formula and how it can be used to solve problems on the coordinate plane.

The distance formula is derived from the Pythagorean theorem, which states that in a right triangle, the square of the length of the hypotenuse (the side opposite the right angle) is equal to the sum of the squares of the lengths of the other two sides. If we consider a line segment AB with endpoints A(x1, y1) and B(x2, y2) on the coordinate plane, we can form a right triangle with AB as the hypotenuse.

The horizontal side of the triangle is the difference in the x-coordinates of A and B, which is (x2 - x1), and the vertical side is the difference in the y-coordinates, which is (y2 - y1). According to the Pythagorean theorem, the square of the length of AB is equal to the sum of the squares of the lengths of the other two sides. Taking the square root of both sides, we get the distance formula:

$$
d = \sqrt{(x2 - x1)^2 + (y2 - y1)^2}
$$

This formula gives us the distance between any two points on the coordinate plane. It is a powerful tool for solving a wide range of problems in analytic geometry.

For example, we can use the distance formula to find the radius of a circle given its center and a point on the circle, or to find the length of the diagonal of a rectangle given its vertices. We can also use it to prove geometric properties, such as showing that a quadrilateral is a square by proving that all its sides are equal and its diagonals are equal.

In the next subsection, we will look at some example problems that can be solved using the distance formula. Practice using this formula with different pairs of points to get a good understanding of it.

### Section: Problem solving with distance on the coordinate plane

#### Applying the distance formula in problem solving

Now that we have a good understanding of the distance formula, let's apply it to solve some problems. The key to using the distance formula effectively is to correctly identify the coordinates of the points involved and to apply the formula accurately.

##### Example 1: Finding the radius of a circle

Suppose we have a circle with its center at the point C(2, 3) and a point P(5, 7) on the circle. We can find the radius of the circle by calculating the distance between C and P.

Using the distance formula:

$$
r = \sqrt{(x2 - x1)^2 + (y2 - y1)^2}
$$

Substituting the coordinates of C and P:

$$
r = \sqrt{(5 - 2)^2 + (7 - 3)^2} = \sqrt{9 + 16} = \sqrt{25} = 5
$$

So, the radius of the circle is 5 units.

##### Example 2: Proving a quadrilateral is a square

Suppose we have a quadrilateral ABCD with vertices A(1, 1), B(4, 1), C(4, 4), and D(1, 4). We can prove that ABCD is a square by showing that all its sides are equal and its diagonals are equal.

First, we calculate the lengths of the sides using the distance formula:

AB = $\sqrt{(4 - 1)^2 + (1 - 1)^2} = \sqrt{9} = 3$

BC = $\sqrt{(4 - 4)^2 + (4 - 1)^2} = \sqrt{9} = 3$

CD = $\sqrt{(1 - 4)^2 + (4 - 4)^2} = \sqrt{9} = 3$

DA = $\sqrt{(1 - 1)^2 + (1 - 4)^2} = \sqrt{9} = 3$

Since all the sides are equal, ABCD is a rhombus. Now, we calculate the lengths of the diagonals:

AC = $\sqrt{(4 - 1)^2 + (4 - 1)^2} = \sqrt{18} = 3\sqrt{2}$

BD = $\sqrt{(1 - 4)^2 + (4 - 1)^2} = \sqrt{18} = 3\sqrt{2}$

Since the diagonals are equal, ABCD is a rectangle. Since ABCD is both a rhombus and a rectangle, it is a square.

These examples illustrate how the distance formula can be used to solve problems in analytic geometry. Practice solving problems using the distance formula to become more proficient in its application.

### Section: Parallel & perpendicular lines on the coordinate plane

In this section, we will explore the concepts of parallel and perpendicular lines on the coordinate plane. These concepts are fundamental to analytic geometry and will be used frequently in problem-solving.

#### Understanding parallel lines

Parallel lines are lines in a plane that do not intersect. They remain the same distance apart over their entire length. In the context of a coordinate plane, parallel lines have the same slope. 

Let's consider two lines, L1: $y = m_1x + c_1$ and L2: $y = m_2x + c_2$. These lines are parallel if and only if $m_1 = m_2$. The y-intercepts, $c_1$ and $c_2$, do not affect whether the lines are parallel.

##### Example 1: Identifying parallel lines

Consider the lines L1: $y = 2x + 3$ and L2: $y = 2x - 1$. Since the slopes of both lines are equal (m = 2), these lines are parallel.

#### Understanding perpendicular lines

Perpendicular lines are lines that intersect at a right angle (90 degrees). In the context of a coordinate plane, two lines are perpendicular if the product of their slopes is -1.

Let's consider two lines, L1: $y = m_1x + c_1$ and L2: $y = m_2x + c_2$. These lines are perpendicular if and only if $m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$.

##### Example 2: Identifying perpendicular lines

Consider the lines L1: $y = 3x + 2$ and L2: $y = -\frac{1}{3}x - 1$. Since the product of the slopes is -1 (3 * -1/3 = -1), these lines are perpendicular.

Understanding parallel and perpendicular lines is crucial in analytic geometry. It helps in determining the relationships between different lines on the coordinate plane. Practice identifying parallel and perpendicular lines to become more proficient in their recognition.

#### Identifying parallel and perpendicular lines on the coordinate plane

In the previous section, we learned about the properties of parallel and perpendicular lines and how to identify them based on their slopes. Now, let's apply this knowledge to identify parallel and perpendicular lines on the coordinate plane.

##### Example 3: Identifying parallel lines on the coordinate plane

Consider the lines L1: $y = 4x + 1$ and L2: $y = 4x - 5$. 

To determine if these lines are parallel, we need to compare their slopes. The slope of L1 is 4, and the slope of L2 is also 4. Since the slopes are equal, we can conclude that these lines are parallel.

##### Example 4: Identifying perpendicular lines on the coordinate plane

Consider the lines L1: $y = -2x + 3$ and L2: $y = \frac{1}{2}x - 1$.

To determine if these lines are perpendicular, we need to check if the product of their slopes is -1. The slope of L1 is -2, and the slope of L2 is 1/2. The product of the slopes is -2 * 1/2 = -1. Therefore, these lines are perpendicular.

##### Practice Problems

1. Are the lines $y = 5x + 2$ and $y = 5x - 3$ parallel, perpendicular, or neither? 
2. Are the lines $y = -3x + 1$ and $y = \frac{1}{3}x + 2$ parallel, perpendicular, or neither? 
3. Are the lines $y = 2x + 4$ and $y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 1$ parallel, perpendicular, or neither?

Understanding how to identify parallel and perpendicular lines on the coordinate plane is a fundamental skill in analytic geometry. It allows us to analyze the relationships between different lines and shapes on the plane. Practice these problems to become more proficient in identifying parallel and perpendicular lines.

### Section: Equations of parallel & perpendicular lines

In the previous section, we learned how to identify parallel and perpendicular lines on the coordinate plane by comparing their slopes. Now, we will learn how to find the equations of lines that are parallel or perpendicular to a given line.

#### Understanding equations of parallel lines

Two lines are parallel if and only if they have the same slope. This means that if we have a line with equation $y = mx + b$, any line parallel to this line will have the equation $y = mx + c$, where $m$ is the slope and $c$ is any real number.

##### Example 1: Finding the equation of a line parallel to a given line

Let's say we have a line with equation $y = 3x + 2$, and we want to find the equation of a line that is parallel to this line and passes through the point (1, 2).

Since parallel lines have the same slope, the slope of the new line will also be 3. Using the point-slope form of a line, $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$, where $(x_1, y_1)$ is the point the line passes through and $m$ is the slope, we can find the equation of the new line:

$$y - 2 = 3(x - 1)$$
$$y = 3x + 1$$

So, the equation of the line parallel to $y = 3x + 2$ that passes through the point (1, 2) is $y = 3x + 1$.

#### Understanding equations of perpendicular lines

Two lines are perpendicular if and only if the product of their slopes is -1. This means that if we have a line with equation $y = mx + b$, any line perpendicular to this line will have the equation $y = -\frac{1}{m}x + c$, where $-\frac{1}{m}$ is the slope of the perpendicular line and $c$ is any real number.

##### Example 2: Finding the equation of a line perpendicular to a given line

Let's say we have a line with equation $y = 2x + 3$, and we want to find the equation of a line that is perpendicular to this line and passes through the point (2, 1).

Since the slope of the given line is 2, the slope of the line perpendicular to it will be $-\frac{1}{2}$. Using the point-slope form of a line, we can find the equation of the new line:

$$y - 1 = -\frac{1}{2}(x - 2)$$
$$y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 2$$

So, the equation of the line perpendicular to $y = 2x + 3$ that passes through the point (2, 1) is $y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 2$.

##### Practice Problems

1. Find the equation of the line parallel to $y = 4x - 1$ that passes through the point (3, 2).
2. Find the equation of the line perpendicular to $y = -3x + 2$ that passes through the point (-1, 1).
3. Find the equation of the line parallel to $y = \frac{1}{2}x + 1$ that passes through the point (0, -1).
4. Find the equation of the line perpendicular to $y = 5x - 3$ that passes through the point (2, -2).

Understanding how to find the equations of lines that are parallel or perpendicular to a given line is a crucial skill in analytic geometry. It allows us to explore the relationships between different lines and shapes on the coordinate plane. Practice these problems to become more proficient in finding the equations of parallel and perpendicular lines.

ne, $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$, we can find the equation of the new line:

$$y - 1 = -\frac{1}{2}(x - 2)$$
$$y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 2$$

So, the equation of the line perpendicular to $y = 2x + 3$ that passes through the point (2, 1) is $y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 2$.

### Subsection: Writing equations of parallel and perpendicular lines

Now that we understand how to find the equations of lines that are parallel or perpendicular to a given line, let's practice writing these equations ourselves.

#### Example 3: Writing the equation of a line parallel to a given line

Suppose we have a line with equation $y = -4x + 5$, and we want to write the equation of a line that is parallel to this line and passes through the point (3, -1).

Since parallel lines have the same slope, the slope of the new line will also be -4. Using the point-slope form of a line, $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$, we can write the equation of the new line:

$$y - (-1) = -4(x - 3)$$
$$y + 1 = -4x + 12$$
$$y = -4x + 11$$

So, the equation of the line parallel to $y = -4x + 5$ that passes through the point (3, -1) is $y = -4x + 11$.

#### Example 4: Writing the equation of a line perpendicular to a given line

Suppose we have a line with equation $y = \frac{1}{3}x + 2$, and we want to write the equation of a line that is perpendicular to this line and passes through the point (1, 1).

Since the slope of the given line is $\frac{1}{3}$, the slope of the line perpendicular to it will be $-\frac{1}{\frac{1}{3}} = -3$. Using the point-slope form of a line, $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$, we can write the equation of the new line:

$$y - 1 = -3(x - 1)$$
$$y = -3x + 4$$

So, the equation of the line perpendicular to $y = \frac{1}{3}x + 2$ that passes through the point (1, 1) is $y = -3x + 4$.

In conclusion, to write the equation of a line that is parallel or perpendicular to a given line, we need to remember that parallel lines have the same slope and the slopes of perpendicular lines are negative reciprocals of each other. Using the point-slope form of a line, we can then substitute the appropriate slope and the coordinates of the point the line passes through to find the equation of the line.

## Chapter: Analytic geometry

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Analytic Geometry in the book "Mastering Integrated Math 1." In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts and techniques of analytic geometry, which is a branch of mathematics that combines algebra and geometry. 

Analytic geometry provides a powerful framework for studying geometric shapes and their properties using algebraic equations. By representing geometric objects as equations, we can analyze their characteristics, relationships, and transformations in a precise and systematic manner. This approach allows us to solve problems involving lines, circles, parabolas, ellipses, and other curves, as well as investigate the connections between algebraic equations and geometric shapes.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into various topics related to analytic geometry. We will start by introducing the Cartesian coordinate system, which forms the foundation of analytic geometry. We will learn how to plot points, identify the coordinates of points, and understand the relationship between points and their distances.

Next, we will explore lines and their equations. We will learn how to determine the slope and intercept of a line, as well as how to write the equation of a line in different forms, such as slope-intercept form and point-slope form. We will also investigate parallel and perpendicular lines and explore their properties.

Moving on, we will study circles and their equations. We will learn how to identify the center and radius of a circle, as well as how to write the equation of a circle in standard form. We will also explore the properties of tangents and secants to circles and examine their relationships with the circle's equation.

In addition to lines and circles, we will delve into conic sections, which are curves formed by the intersection of a plane and a cone. We will study parabolas, ellipses, and hyperbolas, and learn how to write their equations in standard form. We will investigate their key properties, such as the focus, directrix, and eccentricity, and explore how changes in the equation affect the shape and orientation of these curves.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the principles and techniques of analytic geometry. You will be able to confidently analyze and solve problems involving geometric shapes and their equations, and you will have a strong foundation for further studies in mathematics and related fields. So let's dive in and master the world of analytic geometry!

### Section: Equations of parallel & perpendicular lines

In this section, we will explore the equations of parallel and perpendicular lines. Understanding how to write these equations is essential for analyzing and solving problems involving lines in analytic geometry.

#### Writing equations of parallel lines

Two lines are said to be parallel if they never intersect. Parallel lines have the same slope but different y-intercepts. To write the equation of a line parallel to another line, we need to know the slope of the given line and a point that lies on the line.

Let's say we have a line with equation $y = mx + b$, where $m$ is the slope and $b$ is the y-intercept. To find the equation of a line parallel to this line, we can use the same slope $m$ and a different y-intercept $c$. The equation of the parallel line can be written as:

$$y = mx + c$$

For example, if we have a line with equation $y = 2x + 3$, a line parallel to this line could have an equation like $y = 2x + 5$.

#### Writing equations of perpendicular lines

Two lines are said to be perpendicular if they intersect at a right angle. Perpendicular lines have slopes that are negative reciprocals of each other. To write the equation of a line perpendicular to another line, we need to know the slope of the given line and a point that lies on the line.

Let's say we have a line with equation $y = mx + b$. To find the equation of a line perpendicular to this line, we can use the negative reciprocal of the slope $m$ and a different y-intercept $c$. The equation of the perpendicular line can be written as:

$$y = -\frac{1}{m}x + c$$

For example, if we have a line with equation $y = 2x + 3$, a line perpendicular to this line could have an equation like $y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 5$.

It's important to note that when writing equations of parallel and perpendicular lines, we can choose any point on the line to determine the y-intercept. The slope, however, must be consistent with the given conditions.

Now that we have learned how to write equations of parallel and perpendicular lines, let's practice applying these concepts with some examples and exercises.

## Chapter: Analytic geometry

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Analytic Geometry in the book "Mastering Integrated Math 1." In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts and techniques of analytic geometry, which is a branch of mathematics that combines algebra and geometry. Analytic geometry provides a powerful framework for studying geometric shapes and their properties using algebraic equations and coordinates.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into various topics that form the foundation of analytic geometry. We will start by introducing the Cartesian coordinate system, which allows us to represent points, lines, and curves using numerical coordinates. By understanding the coordinate system, we can easily visualize and analyze geometric objects in a systematic manner.

Next, we will explore the concept of equations of lines. We will learn how to determine the equation of a line given its slope and a point on the line, as well as how to find the slope of a line given its equation. We will also discuss different forms of linear equations, such as slope-intercept form and point-slope form, and how to convert between them.

Moving on, we will study the properties of circles and their equations. We will learn how to find the equation of a circle given its center and radius, as well as how to determine the center and radius of a circle given its equation. We will also explore the relationship between circles and lines, including tangent lines and secant lines.

In addition to lines and circles, we will investigate other conic sections, such as parabolas, ellipses, and hyperbolas. We will learn how to identify the key features of these curves, including their foci, vertices, and directrices. We will also examine the equations that describe these conic sections and how to manipulate them to obtain the desired information.

Throughout this chapter, we will provide numerous examples and practice problems to reinforce your understanding of the concepts and techniques discussed. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid foundation in analytic geometry and be able to confidently apply its principles to solve a wide range of mathematical problems. So let's dive in and master the world of analytic geometry!

### Section: Equations of parallel & perpendicular lines

In this section, we will explore how to write equations of parallel and perpendicular lines. Understanding these concepts is crucial in analytic geometry as it allows us to determine the relationship between different lines and analyze their properties.

#### Writing equations of parallel lines

Two lines are said to be parallel if they never intersect. Parallel lines have the same slope but different y-intercepts. To write the equation of a line parallel to another line, we can use the slope-intercept form of a linear equation, which is given by:

$$
y = mx + b
$$

where $m$ represents the slope of the line and $b$ represents the y-intercept.

To find the equation of a line parallel to another line, we need to determine the slope of the given line. Once we have the slope, we can use it to write the equation of the parallel line.

Let's consider an example to illustrate this concept:

Example: Find the equation of a line parallel to the line $y = 2x + 3$ passing through the point (4, 5).

Solution: Since the given line has a slope of 2, the parallel line will also have a slope of 2. Using the point-slope form of a linear equation, we can write the equation of the parallel line as:

$$
y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)
$$

where $(x_1, y_1)$ represents the coordinates of the given point.

Substituting the values from the given point, we have:

$$
y - 5 = 2(x - 4)
$$

Simplifying the equation, we get:

$$
y - 5 = 2x - 8
$$

$$
y = 2x - 3
$$

Therefore, the equation of the line parallel to $y = 2x + 3$ passing through the point (4, 5) is $y = 2x - 3$.

#### Writing equations of perpendicular lines

Two lines are said to be perpendicular if they intersect at a right angle. Perpendicular lines have slopes that are negative reciprocals of each other. To write the equation of a line perpendicular to another line, we can again use the slope-intercept form of a linear equation.

To find the equation of a line perpendicular to another line, we need to determine the slope of the given line. Once we have the slope, we can find the negative reciprocal of it and use it to write the equation of the perpendicular line.

Let's consider an example to illustrate this concept:

Example: Find the equation of a line perpendicular to the line $y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 2$ passing through the point (3, 4).

Solution: The given line has a slope of $-\frac{1}{2}$. The perpendicular line will have a slope that is the negative reciprocal of $-\frac{1}{2}$, which is $\frac{2}{1}$ or simply 2. Using the point-slope form of a linear equation, we can write the equation of the perpendicular line as:

$$
y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)
$$

Substituting the values from the given point, we have:

$$
y - 4 = 2(x - 3)
$$

Simplifying the equation, we get:

$$
y - 4 = 2x - 6
$$

$$
y = 2x - 2
$$

Therefore, the equation of the line perpendicular to $y = -\frac{1}{2}x + 2$ passing through the point (3, 4) is $y = 2x - 2$.

In this section, we have learned how to write equations of parallel and perpendicular lines. These concepts are essential in analyzing the relationships between different lines and understanding their properties. Practice solving more examples to strengthen your understanding of this topic.
